For indeed, if Aristotle (the philosopher) in our language is named ‘Aristotle’ and any 
language in which ‘Aristotle’ does not refer to (our) Aristotle is not our language, then, 
insofar as we speak in our own language, it is not possible that Aristotle is such a person 
that it might not have been (called in our language) ‘Aristotle’. However, certainly, it is 
possible that there is a language in which Aristotle is not called ‘Aristotle’. 
2.3 Existential propositions 
The 
properties 
of 
existential 
propositions 
was 
the 
ﬁrst 
issue 
on 
which 
Le´sniewski published (1911) and to which he applied his conventions. In this paper 
Le´sniewski claimed that: (i) all positive and all negative existential propositions are false, 
whereas (ii) all positive are analytic (so, analyticity does not imply truth, according to 
Le´sniewski), (iii) some negative propositions (with contradictory subjects) are analytic, 
and (iv) some negative propositions are synthetic. 
Existential propositions are deﬁned as atomic propositions of a very speciﬁc form. A 
positive existential proposition is of the form: ‘S exists’. Or equivalently: ‘S is a being’ 
(1911: 3). A negative existential proposition has the form: ‘S does not exist’ or ‘S is not 
a being’ where ‘being’ is a noun meaning the same as Latin ens, Greek on, or English 
object. 
Now, following the convention regarding connoting expressions (2.1), we know that 
‘being’ connotes something only if it can be deﬁned by means of a classical deﬁnition. 

33 
However, to construct such a deﬁnition, we would have to ﬁnd such B and C that: 
(2.22) 
Being is a B which is C. 
would be a classical deﬁnition. To make such a deﬁnition work, B would have to be a 
name wider than ‘being’. However, (and that is another concept taken from Aristotelian 
thought) there is no name wider than ‘being’ or ‘object’, for everything that can be 
named is an object, and as such, a being of some sort.19 
Thus, there is no deﬁnition of ‘being’, and the name ‘being’ does not connote anything. 
This being the case, in accordance with the convention regarding analyticity (2.15), it 
follows that: 
(2.23) 
All positive existential propositions are analytic. 
Recall that a positive atomic proposition is analytic if and only if its predicate does not 
connote properties which are not connoted by the subject. Since ‘being’ does not connote 
anything, it is certain that whatever is the subject of a positive existential proposition, 
its predicate does not connote anything which is not connoted by the subject; thus, every 
positive existential proposition is analytic.20 
Nevertheless, every positive existential proposition is, according to Le´sniewski, false. 
His justiﬁcation of this claim is based on the convention on the truth-conditions of atomic 
propositions (2.9). Indeed, ‘S is a being’ is true iﬀ S has all properties connoted by 
‘being’. But, since there are no such properties, it is false that S has them. The fact that 
19“I have said that the predicate of a positive existential proposition which has been brought to the 
form of a proposition with a positive copula, does not connote anything, except — at most — the 
property of being greater than one. I maintain this because such a predicate is synonymous with the 
words ‘being’ or ‘beings’ which connote nothing else, even though they denote (‘denotation’ as used by 
Mill) everything. This view conﬂicts with J.S. Mill’s theory which says the word ‘being’ connotes the 
property of existing. I consider Mill’s theory wrong because, should the word ‘being’ really connote 
the property of existing, we could deﬁne that word as ‘that which has the property of existing’, or 
in other words, as a ‘being which has the property of existing’ (since the deﬁnition must indicate not 
only diﬀerentiae speciﬁcae, but also the genus); this would, then, give rise to an inevitable regressus 
in inﬁnitum. The word ‘being’ cannot be in fact deﬁned at all; the statement that this word does not 
connote anything is fully in keeping with this fact.” (1911: 4–5) 
20This is the main line of Le´sniewski’s argument for this claim. 

34 
Le´sniewski thought this argument sound indicates that he treated a sentence of the form 
‘all A are B’ as implying the existence of some A’s. If, instead, (2.9) was read as saying 
that it is true iﬀ no properties connoted by the predicate are not had by the object(s) 
denoted by the subject, the argument would not work. 
Now consider negative existential propositions. There are two options. A negative 
existential proposition is either analytic or it is synthetic. 
If S itself connotes the property of non-being, then ‘S is a non-being’ is analytic, and 
yet false. According to Le´sniewski, atomic propositions with contradictory subjects are 
contradictory themselves, and cannot be true (2.11). 
If ‘S’ does not connote the property of non-being, ‘S is a non-being’ is synthetic 
and the S is only said to have the property of non-being. But every ‘S’ is reducible to a 
name phrase ‘being which has properties A1, A2, A3, . . .’. So we have a proposition saying 
something equivalent to: 
(2.24) 
The being which has properties A1, A2, A3, . . ., is not a being. 
which is a contradictory proposition. 
Indeed, Le´sniewski’s conclusions seem to be quite awkward. He noticed this awk­
wardness and commented on it. It is interesting to see what he said about it. 
So, ﬁrst, it might be objected that the deﬁnitions that he uses in proving his con­
clusions are artiﬁcial. For Le´sniewski, the relevance of a classiﬁcation depends on its 
theoretical usefulness, i.e. on whether it allows us either to construct propositions or 
theories concerning all and only those objects which fall under one of the classes that 
our classiﬁcation singles out. Now, his classiﬁcation makes it easier to put forward some 
theoretical claims concerning all and only (simple) analytic propositions and other claims 
concerning all and only (simple) synthetic propositions. Some examples that Le´sniewski 
gave were: ‘All analytic propositions (and only these propositions) contain no such in­
formation about the objects symbolized by the subjects, that could not be deduced from 

35 
the meaning of the subjects’ and ‘All synthetic propositions (and only these propositions) 
contain such information about the objects symbolized by the subjects’. (1911: 9–10)21 
So, one may think, what Le´sniewski did is introduce a bunch of deﬁnitions which, 
together with some assumptions about how the language works, implied some claims that 
may sound bizarre. But they only sound awkward, because the terms that are used in 
those claims are taken in a diﬀerent meaning than the meaning that we usually associate 
with those terms. If I say ‘I will use the term ‘cow’ instead of the term ‘bat’, I will be 
committed to saying that ‘cows have wings’ and even though it sounds weird, it is true. 
Le´sniewski, however, treated his conclusions as if they actually had some bearing on 
what is true in the natural language. He seriously thought that his claims (even though 
they resulted from quite unusual deﬁnitions) held for the ordinary language. Here is 
what he says about that: 
The idea expressed in the preceding section contradicts current opinions con­
cerning existential propositions: the possibility of constructing both positive 
and negative, true existential propositions is commonly accepted. Thus e.g., 
21About the idea that some classiﬁcations are natural and some are artiﬁcial he says: “One hears, 
from time to time, of ‘natural’ and ‘artiﬁcial’ methods of classiﬁcation. People who use this form of 
expression do not usually limit themselves in characterizing particular methods of classiﬁcation, to the 
inclusion of these descriptively to either of the above categories; they usually combine such a descriptive 
characterization of methods of classiﬁcation with the teleological element of valuation, and they value 
‘natural’ classiﬁcations higher than artiﬁcial’ ones. The origins of the above characterizations of methods 
of classiﬁcation, and the positive or negative estimations which accompany these characterizations, can 
vary immensely from case to case. 
Some such cases are determined by various linguistic habits and traditions, others — by more or less 
well thought out and justiﬁed views concerning the problems of theoretical usefulness. 
The classiﬁcation of propositions into analytic and synthetic which I have carried out . . . can be, in view 
of at least one of its consequences, characterized as regarded by some as ‘artiﬁcial’. Such an ‘objection’ 
can in the ﬁrst place originate from the fact that one of the two classiﬁcation labels, i.e., that of analytic 
propositions, comprises two ‘very’ or ‘too’ heterogenous groups of propositions: (1) propositions whose 
predicates connote any properties but not those connoted by the subjects’, and (2) propositions whose 
predicates do not connote the properties connoted by the subjects only because they do not connote any 
features. 
I do not consider it my task to tone down all such dissonances if they arise solely from deeply rooted 
emotional impulses resulting from some linguistic habits, yet I cannot miss the opportunity to provide 
my classiﬁcation of propositions with a ‘safety valve’ against objections supported by arguments of 
theoretical usefulness of my classiﬁcation.” (1911: 9) 

36 
both the proposition ‘people exist’ and the proposition ‘square circles do not 
exist’ are considered true. 
I have taken pains to demonstrate the groundlessness of this common attitude 
towards existential propositions, all that remains for me to do is to cast some 
light on the probable origin of such a widespread error. [emphasis mine] 
(1911: 15) 
He also suggested that his claims about analytic propositions were claims about the 
same group of propositions which was referred to by other logicians and philosophers: 
In modern logic there is a widespread conviction that all analytic propositions 
are true. Those who advocate such a privileged position of analytic proposi­
tions in science claim that the principle of contradiction would be in jeopardy 
if any proposition could be false in spite of its being analytic [. . . But] some 
analytic propositions might be true, while others might be false. [. . . ] Thus, 
those scholars who hold that all analytic propositions are necessarily true 
propositions are in error. (1913b: 61–62) 
Next, he points out that sentences that seem to be true and existential are mis­
represented and their “adequate verbal symbols would be non-existential propositions” 
(1911: 15), that is, instead of ‘S exist(s)’, if we take it to be true, we should use ‘Some 
being(s) is(/are) S’ and by ‘S does not exist’ we should use ‘No being(s) is(/are) S’, if 
we want to represent our beliefs and intentions adequately.22 
There is something speciﬁc to note about his arguments. His reasoning for the claim 
that all positive existential propositions are analytic assumes that ‘being’ does not con­
note anything (for it is not deﬁnable by means of a classical deﬁnition). On the other 
22Although his explanation of how he got to this conclusions is somewhat awkward: “The semiotic 
analysis of the adequacy or inadequacy of certain propositions in relation to the contents which they 
represent is then ultimately based on a phenomenological analysis of the speaker’s representational 
intentions.” (1911: 17) 

37 
hand, his justiﬁcation of the claim that negative propositions are false assumes that ‘non­
being’ connotes something. It is far from obvious that by negating a predicate which does 
not connote something one can get a predicate connoting something.23 
A possible objection to this claim is this. It is possible to take any two disjoint B and 
C in order to obtain such a deﬁnition. The problem with this objection is that Le´sniewski 
accepted the classical notion of deﬁnition: B has to be a genus proximum and C has to 
be a diﬀerentia speciﬁca. Obviously this would be impossible had B and C been disjoint. 
2.4 The principle of contradiction 
In 1910,  Lukasiewicz published a book titled On the Principle of Contradiction in Aris­
totle, 24 in which he analyzed Aristotle’s position on the principle of contradiction. This 
led him to distinguishing at least three diﬀerent principles: (i) the logical principle of 
contradiction: 
(2.25) 
For no p : p and not−p are true simultaneously. 
(ii) the ontological principle of contradiction: 
(2.26) 
No being may possess and not possess at the same time one and the 
same quality. 
(iii) the doxastic principle of contradiction: 
(2.27) 
It is not possible to believe in a contradiction. 
23Le´sniewski appears to make two of the standard moves that were usually made those days. (i) He 
suggested that the surface form of a statement does not have to express its content adequately. (ii) 
He used quantiﬁcational devices to express existence. Although (i) seems to be a correct observation, 
Le´sniewski does not seem to have been inﬂuenced by a similar strategy of Russell’s or Frege’s. Probably, 
the extent to which he was acquainted with the western logical thought did not exceed a relatively simple 
formal theory given by  Lukasiewicz in his book on the principle of contradiction ( Lukasiewicz 1910). As 
to (ii), it is unclear which part of ‘Some object is S’ does the heavy lifting of existential import. In his 
early work, Le´sniewski was unclear about that. Later on, however, it was the copula ‘is’ that imported 
the existence rather than the quantiﬁcation. So, for example, it is a theorem of one of Le´sniewski’s 
systems that ∃a¬ex(a), i.e. ‘For some a, a does not exist’. 
24Which still has not been translated from Polish. 

38 
He argued that (2.27) as a factual statement is false (although it may have some worth 
as a methodological directive), and that Aristotle’s arguments for (2.25) and (2.26) are 
not conclusive. 
In his paper from 1912, Le´sniewski, against  Lukasiewicz, attempted a proof of the 
(ontological) principle of contradiction. The basic version in which he formulated that 
principle was: 
(2.28) No being can both possess and not possess one and the same property. 
Which was also equivalently stated as: 
(2.29) 
Every object is unable to both possess and not possess one and the 
same property. 
(2.30) 
Every P is unable to both have and not have c. 
The expression ‘both to possess and not to possess a property c’ will be abbreviated 
as ‘to be a contradiction’. ‘The expression ‘P is unable to be a contradiction’ was read 
by Le´sniewski as equivalent to a conjunction of two claims: 
(2.31) 
P is not a contradiction. 
(2.32) 
The sentence ‘P is not a contradiction’ is true a priori. 
where being true a priori, as it has already been said in the section on conventions (2.13), 
means being provable on the basis of linguistic conventions only. 
Le´sniewski’s argument for the Principle of Contradiction goes as follows: 
(2.33) ‘Some object is a contradiction’ is a contradictory proposition. 
By one of the conventions (regarding contradictory propositions), the sentence ‘Some 
object is a contradiction’ does not possess a symbolic function (2.11). However, it has 
a denoting subject and connoting predicate. Therefore, by the convention regarding the 

39 
truth and falsehood of contradicting propositions with denoting subjects and connoting 
predicates (2.10), the proposition: 
(2.34) 
Every object is not a contradiction. 
is true. Since the proof is based on the linguistic conventions only, the Principle of 
Contradiction is true a priori. 
Clearly, in some reasonable sense of the word ‘proof’, what Le´sniewski gave was a 
proof of the ontological Principle of Contradiction. Nevertheless, one can easily object 
that epistemically speaking the proof is not deeply satisfying; for the conventions on 
which it is based, especially that which says that no contradictory proposition possesses 
a symbolic function, seem to do the heavy lifting and to smuggle the conclusion in already. 
In other words, if someone rejects the ontological Principle of Contradiction, he probably 
will not accept the claim that contradictory propositions do not have a symbolic function 
(still, one has the sneaking suspicion that this convention is just the logical principle of 
contradiction in disguise); thus the main objection against this proof is that it is hard to 
see how it may convince anyone who does not believe in the Principle of Contradiction 
already. 
2.5 The principle of excluded middle 
Le´sniewski (1913b) makes a distinction between two formulations of the Principle of 
Excluded Middle (PEM). One refers to objects (the metaphysical PEM): 
(2.35) Every object has to stand, with respect to every property, either in 
the relation of possessing it or in the relation of not possessing it. 
the second refers to propositions (logical PEM): 
(2.36) At least one of two contradictory propositions has to be true.25 
25Although Le´sniewski did not give a deﬁnition of the contradictory of a sentence, from his discussion 

40 
He clearly states that his rejection of PEM will be the rejection of the logical version. 
He speaks of this version of PEM in not very favorable terms: 
The logical principle of the excluded middle not only does not help to resolve 
‘logical’ problems of various kinds, but it is in fact a dangerous theoretical 
obstacle which should be, therefore, removed from science. (1913b: 47) 
The argument against the logical version of PEM is: 
According to the convention regarding truth-conditions (2.9), for an atomic proposi­
tion of the kind ‘A is B’ to be true, there must be an object denoted by A, having all the 
properties connoted by B (recall that the reading of ‘all properties’ is strong here, so: ‘all 
properties and at least one property’). Hence, every atomic proposition whose subject 
does not denote anything is a false proposition, and every proposition whose predicate 
does not connote anything is a false proposition. 
Thus, contradictory sentences from the following pairs are false: 
(2.37) Every centaur has a tail. Some centaur does not have a tail. 
(2.38) Every square circle is a circle. Some square circle is not a circle. 
(2.39) Pegasus is an animal. Pegasus is not an animal. 
The ﬁrst two examples come from Le´sniewski, the last one does not (but still, given 
his truth conditions for simple propositions, it is also a good example).26 Since these 
it seems that this was a syntactic notion: if S is taken to be a singular term, the sentence contradictory 
with ‘S is P’ would be ‘S is not P’, and if S is taken to be a general term, then the sentence contradictory 
to ‘Some S is P ’ is ‘Every S is not P ’. 
26“Let us suppose that I am to answer the question of whether the following propositions are true: 
‘every centaur has a tail’, ‘a certain centaur does not have a tail’, ‘every square circle is a circle’, ‘a 
certain square circle is not a circle’. If we take into account the above analysis, the answer to this 
question becomes quite easy. Each of the four mentioned propositions is obviously false because the 
subject of each denotes nothing. The word ‘centaur’ which is the subject in the ﬁrst two propositions, 
and the expression ‘square circle’ being the subject in the remaining two — denote nothing because no 
object is a centaur and no object is a square circle. Thus, no object is such that it could be denoted only 
by the word ‘centaur’ or by the expression ‘square circle’. These expressions denote no objects, that is 
to say — they denote nothing.” (1913b: 59) 

41 
pairs are, according to Le´sniewski, pairs of contradictory propositions, they are coun­
terexamples to PEM. 
Thus, Le´sniewski rejects the equivalence: 
(2.40) (‘A is B’ is false) ≡ (A is not B)27 
Moreover, this account yields some speciﬁc examples of false analytic sentences: 
(2.41) Every contradictory object is contradictory. 
(2.42) A square circle is a circle. 
Note that according to Le´sniewski, the proposition contradictory to ‘S is P ’ is ‘S is not 
a P ’, which is not equivalent to ‘It is not the case that S is P .’ To some extent it allows 
one to think that his discussion is not a discussion of the Principle of Excluded Middle 
proper. Nevertheless, we will later see that the results given in this paper (however one 
would like to call them) will be important for further discussion (for example, for his 
solution of Russell’s paradox). Also, this issue allows us to see the diﬀerence between the 
metaphysical PEM and the logical PEM. Le´sniewski’s rejection of logical PEM does not 
undermine the metaphysical version; Le´sniewski still claimed that any existing object 
either has or does not have a given property, and the only case where the logical PEM 
might fail is when a subject of a sentence fails to denote.28 
27“The points raised . . . throw some light on the ‘problem’ of negative propositions. They demonstrate 
the falsehood of the theory of negative propositions, developed in considerable detail by Sigwart in his 
Logic and defended by some other modern logicians. According to this theory — the negative proposition 
‘A is not B’ is equivalent to the aﬃrmative proposition ‘the proposition ‘A is B’ is false’. . . Given the 
propositions ‘the centaur has no tail’, ‘a square circle is not a circle’ . . . the respective propositions . . . are 
‘the proposition ‘the centaur has a tail’ is false’ and ‘the proposition ‘the square circle is a circle’ is false’. 
The propositions of the type ‘A is not B’ . . . are in this case false because . . . they have subjects which 
denote nothing . . . For the same reason, the respective propositions of the type ‘A is B’, i.e. ‘the centaur 
has a tail’, ‘a square circle is a circle’ — are also false. If, however, the last two propositions are false, 
then the propositions stating their falsehood must be true.” (1913b: 59–60) 
28Even though Le´sniewski rejected one of the forms of the PEM, he did not divide sentences into 
true, false and indeterminate — according to him, if a PEM failure takes place, it is because both 
a sentence and its negation are false. Gelber (2004) suggested the following: “On the basis of his 
interpretation of Aristotle, for instance, Le´sniewski developed a three-valued classiﬁcation with true, 
false and indeterminate as the values” (p. 231). This is not Le´sniewski’s view. The charitable reading 
of Gelber is that Le´sniewski is there mistaken with  Lukasiewicz). 

42 
2.6 Eternity of truth 
Le´sniewski believed that any sentence that was true is and will be true, and that any 
sentence that was false is and will be false. In this sense, he believed in the eternity of 
truth. Unfortunately, he did not write a paper where he explains his views on this issue. 
Rather, much has to be read oﬀ from a paper where he criticized Tadeusz Kotarbi´nski, 
who believed that there are some sentences which are neither true nor false, that may 
become true or false with the ﬂow of time. Since Kotarbi´nski’s views are quite important 
for the development of many-valued logics (his views inspired  Lukasiewicz to construct his 
three-valued logic) and Kotarbi´nski’s paper has never been translated from Polish, I will 
start with a lengthy presentation of Kotarbi´nski’s views. Then I will turn to Le´sniewski’s 
criticism of those views. 
In 1913 Tadeusz Kotarbi´nski, a representative of the Lvov-Warsaw school, published 
a paper titled “The problem of the existence of the future.” This paper was far from 
providing clear justiﬁcation. It employs many metaphors, and it is hard to ﬁnd out what 
his arguments really are. What he argues for, however, is that if every sentence is either 
true or false, and sentences do not change their truth-value through time, then all actions 
are predetermined and there is no free will. The main line of reasoning seems to be the 
following.29 
29Here is a sample of his style in that paper: “Truly one has said that what has gone away has never 
ceased to exist — it only came into absence. What happened, truly happened; one who states that, states 
the truth, so this something does exist. To change the past — this is a mad idea. To give somebody 
the world of the past under his reign is to yield an enormous sphere of reality which he cannot reign. If 
impossibilities are prone to gradation, it is more impossible to reverse a yesterday’s ﬂight of a mosquito 
than to modify the usual moon’s trajectory tomorrow. What has happened cannot be undone. But 
really, is it only true about the past? Is it only the past things and events which are such and such, so 
that they cannot be not such and such, or is it also the things that are to come that are already such 
and such and not diﬀerent and they cannot come about as not such and such? Is it also the case that 
also in the future there is something that cannot be undone, because it has already happened? Many 
would ﬁrmly deny that. But after a consideration, having understood the issue, he would equally ﬁrmly 
assert it. And a scientiﬁcally educated man, with his eyes shut is eager to concede without a doubt that 
the whole future is such and such, and even though nothing in it has happened, it nevertheless cannot 
be undone. What is to come, allegedly diﬀers from what has gone by just as, for example, what happens 
now behind us in space diﬀers from what is happening in front of us . . . as a cloud above our heads from 

43 
Suppose it is true that p is a sentence true at t0. Then it is now true that p is true 
at t0. But if that is the case, I cannot do anything to change it, because it has already 
been ‘decided’ that p at t0 is to come. An analogous reasoning seems to apply to false 
sentences. Suppose that p is a sentence false at t0. Then, it is not false that p is true at 
t0. But if it is already so, there is nothing we can do to change it. 
Speaking more in terms of Kotarbi´nski’s terminology, Kotarbi´nski identiﬁes being 
an object of a true aﬃrmative judgment with existence (‘judgment’ is taken from Ko­
tarbi´nski’s terminology). He argues that if a judgement is true at some time t, then it 
is true at any time later than t. It follows, that whatever exists at some time t, exists 
at any time later than t (i.e. never ceases to exist), and whatever does not exist now, 
never comes to existence (for if what p states does not exist, then what not − p states 
does exist and continues do to so). This conclusion seems for him quite problematic. We 
usually believe that we can create something: whether it is a result of our moral action, 
or a subject of our artistic or intellectual activity. Since this (following his deﬁnition of 
existence) cannot be the case (at least he so believes), if the propositions referring to 
those created objects have already been true before the act of creation, he concludes that 
some propositions referring to future freely created objects are neither true nor false. 
This is just a guess about what the argument would have been like if Kotarbi´nski cared 
to formulate it as a clear argument. Here is how he originally phrased his view (again, I 
quote Kotarbi´nski at length since his work has not been translated): 
Truly, an enormous part of the things to come are, practically speaking, has 
more to do with the past than with the other things in the future. The ﬂow 
of sea currents, earthquakes, volcano eruptions, the rotations of heavenly 
bodies cannot be changed. We cannot make it true that golf stream will ﬂow 
through Poland, that the tomorrow’s golf stream’s ﬂow is here. With this 
another cloud above the Paciﬁc Ocean.” [My translation of (Kotarbi´nski,1913: 74)]. 

44 
sort of things we are powerless. No matter whether we leave them behind 
or whether they are in the future. We have to reckon with those to come as 
well as with those that has passed. Those things are such and such and their 
having those properties in the future exists even though they are in the future 
. . . It is already a true judgment which asserts that an object which lasts in 
the future will die. My inherence, pictorially speaking, of the property of 
being dead is true already. But is my every position in the future equally 
existent? Is the fact that I will die equally true as the fact that I will die at 
this speciﬁc time, and that this and no other job I will have, that among two 
roads I will choose the one on the right and not the one on the left, that in 
that speciﬁc moment such and such thought will cross my mind, raised by 
my attention in that moment, that I will make a vow (or not), abide by it 
(or not)? Is this also already true today and was it true centuries ago, or 
at least since my birth? No, never! Those things are undecided, they are in 
our hands, under our reign, and the great practical diﬀerence which divides 
everything in two spheres is not the present moment: it only crosses the 
present moment at some places. Of course, on the other side of the river that 
ﬂows on the border of our freedom, there remains the whole gigantic world 
of things that are ready to come, which already have the truth to accompany 
them. The freedom ends where the truth begins, not where the past comes 
into being. If I can do something, create it, it is not true that it already is. 
For how can one create what is, what happened or what already is created? 
One may, at most, create something similar, but not the very same thing. 
But neither what I can create is false. For how can one come to being, if 
the judgment stating its existence is false? If it came about (as created), we 
would have had a contradiction: a judgment about it would have been true 

45 
and false. Apparently, maybe not, but in fact — indeed. For we are talking 
not about making something which is not present a future thing. We are 
not talking about a microbe infecting a presently healthy man. We are not 
talking about covering a canvas which is now white with paint. We do not 
need creativity (in our ontological sense) for that; and this is what one has 
in mind when he denies intuitively the impossibility of creating something 
when the corresponding judgment about it is false. Only when we truly 
create, we create the truth. . . For, for something to truly come into being it 
is required that before it does, the judgment stating it to be not true. This 
is the condition of creativity. On the other hand, there is no creativity, if 
before that moment where the judgment is to begin to be true, it is false. 
For what is false, cannot become true. If it is false that in the moment t 
an object has a given feature, one cannot make it that this object has this 
feature at t, even if t is a future moment. Otherwise — a contradiction. For 
if one can make this happen, then the assertion of this achievement cannot 
contradict any true judgment, whereas from the assertion of this achievement 
it follows that the assertion of what is achieved is true, that it is true that 
the object has this feature in that moment, but the assumption says that 
it is false. Contradiction. If anyone wanted to argue that the possibility of 
doing something does not presuppose the existence of the result, but rather 
only that the result will exist, and that he does not presuppose the truth 
of the corresponding judgment now, but rather that later, when we perform 
the action, it will be true, one can answer what follows. If it is false that a 
given object has a given feature right now, it will never be true, even when 
the action will have been performed; moreover, it will still be false. So a 
certain judgment will be true and will not be true (it will even be false), so 

46 
— contradiction. For contradiction applies to the future, present and past 
existence equally. So, one cannot leave tomorrow, if we know today that who 
asserts this departure lies. [My translation of: (Kotarbi´nski, 1913: 79–80) 
The most striking feature of Kotarbi´nski’s view is that he identiﬁes truth with neces­
sity and falsehood with impossibility: 
Every truth is a necessity, every falsehood — an impossibility. For what 
is, has to be, because it cannot not be; if something is ready [i.e. already 
decided], but it is not, it cannot be, because already the judgment stating it 
is false, so it cannot become a truth. One can memorize it in a simpler way: 
a judgment is true if the thing is, and therefore when it is necessary, it is false 
— if the thing is impossible and contradictory with something that is.30 
In response to Kotarbi´nski,31 Le´sniewski published a paper: “Is all truth only true 
eternally or is it also true without a beginning?” (1913a). 
Le´sniewski begins with considering Kotarbi´nski’s deﬁnition of existence: 
(2.43) x exists if and only if x is an object such that an aﬃrmative judgment 
referring to it is true. 
Next, he adds some premisses of his own: 
(2.44) Every aﬃrmative judgment asserts that an object possesses a property 
(i.e. a relation of inherence). 
(2.45) 
Being an object to which a judgment refers is the same as being 
asserted by this judgment. 
30“Ka˙zda prawda jest konieczno´sci¸a, ka˙zdy fa lsz — niemo˙zliwo´sci¸a. Gdy co jest bowiem, by´c musi, 
bo nie by´c nie mo˙ze; gdy co´s gotowe jest, a nie jest, to by´c nie mo˙ze, bo ju˙z s¸ad o nim twierdz¸acy 
jest fa lszem, a wi¸ec nie mo˙ze si¸e sta´c prawd¸a. Mo˙zna wi¸ec pro´sciej sobie zapami¸eta´c: s¸ad prawdziwy 
jest, gdy rzecz jest, a wi¸ec gdy jest konieczna, fa lszywy — gdy niemo˙zliwa, sprzeczna z czym´s, co jest.” 
(Kotarbi´nski, 1913: 88–89) 
31Kotarbi´nski’s reasoning resembles the discussion in Peri Hermeneias, IX. 

47 
The conclusion is: 
(2.46) Only relations of inherence can exist. 
which, as he says, seems to contradict Kotarbi´nski, who explicitly stated his own exis­
tence.32 
Discussing Kotarbi´nski’s claim that what is gone by has not ceased to exists, Le´sniewski 
applies an intuitive notion of existence (without giving any deﬁnition) and, assuming that 
a relation of inherence exists at t if and only if it actually takes place at t, points out 
that a relation which has gone by (i.e. ceased to take place) no longer exists. 
Referring to Kotarbi´nski’s account of existence, Le´sniewski argues that an object can 
exist without a judgment referring to it being true, and that there are true judgments 
referring to objects which do not exist. The ﬁrst claim is supported by a nominalistic 
interpretation of Kotarbi´nski’s ‘judgment’: for it is possible that an object exists while 
no sentence-token referring to it exists. The second statement is being shown by means 
of examples of true sentences referring to past (and therefore, no longer existing) objects. 
The same nominalistic account can be found in Le´sniewski’s answer to the question 
of whether all true sentences are true eternally. Literarily speaking about tokens, it is 
doubtful that there are eternally existing tokens: inscriptions, utterances etc. However, 
in a more charitable interpretation of ‘being an eternal truth’, where ‘being true’ means 
‘being true if uttered’, in fact, says Le´sniewski, all true sentences are true eternally. 
32This does not seem to be a very charitable reading of Kotarbi´nski, who seems to have said that there 
are two diﬀerent notions of existence: 
Simply, the term in use is ambiguous. In one sense (which we will use) every object about 
which an aﬃrmative judgment stating it is true, and vice versa: a judgment stating its 
object is true if the object exists . . . in the other sense, the more colloquial one, only the 
present things exist, the past things existed and the future things will exist. 
My translation of the following passage: “Poprostu termin u˙zyty jest dwuznaczny. W jednym sensie, o 
jaki nam chodzi´c b¸edzie, istnieje, jest, ka˙zdy przedmiot, o kt´orym s¸ad twierdz¸acy, stwierdzaj¸acy go, jest 
prawdziwy, i odwrotnie: s¸ad stwierdzaj¸acy przedmiot jest prawdziwy, skoro ten przedmiot istnieje. . . W
