¬
Now the question is how we should understand the negation. The explanation to be 
found in Principia Mathematica is: 
If p is any proposition, the proposition ‘not p’ or ‘p is false’, will be represented 
by ‘ p’.
¬
Thus it seems that according to Whitehead and Russell reading ‘ p’ as either ‘not p’ 
¬
or ‘p is false’ does not change the meaning of what is claimed. This is problematic for 
Le´sniewski, because in ‘p is false’, the symbol p no longer represents a proposition the 
way it does in ‘ p’. When we say ‘ . . . is false’, the dots should be ﬁlled by a name
¬
of a sentence (or proposition), not by the sentence itself. For instance the expression 
‘Spiderman is bald is false’ is not a well-formed sentence. Rather, it has to say something 
about the sentence, as in 
‘Spiderman is bald’ is false. 
That being the case, the sentence:14 
‘p’ is false 
does not mean the same as ‘ p’. They may be equivalent, perhaps, but the former 
¬
says something about a linguistic expression. The latter does not have to do that. The 
expression: ‘not: Spiderman is bald’ is not a sentence about a sentence: it is about 
Spiderman and his baldness (if you will), but it does not contain a name that refers to a 
sentence. 
Similarly, Whitehead and Russell explain the meaning of disjunction: 
If p and q are any propositions, the proposition ‘p or q’, i.e. ‘either p is true 
or q is true’ where the alternatives are to be not mutually exclusive, will be 
represented by ‘p ∨ q’. 
14I put aside the worries about variables occurring in quotations. 

91 
But then (3.5) means the same as: 
(3.6) 
‘ ‘q’ is false’ is true or ‘p or r’ is true. 
which expands to: 
(3.7) 
‘q is false’ is true or ‘ ‘p’ is true or ‘r’ is true’ is true. 
Again, ‘or’ occurs in the above, and since it is taken to mean the same as ‘∨’ we can 
easily climb the ladder of semantic ascent, allegedly preserving the meaning of the whole 
sentence: 
(3.8) 
‘ ‘q’ is false’ is true or either ‘ ‘p’ is true’ is true or ‘ ‘r’ is true’ is true. 
Quite correctly, however, Le´sniewski points out that semantic ascent does not preserve 
the meaning. Propositions that mean the same are about the same. But, for instance, 
‘Paris is in France’ is a sentence about Paris and its location, whereas ‘ ‘Paris is in 
France’ is true’ is about a sentence and its relation to the world. 
In a few words. Indeed, Whitehead and Russell were not perfectly clear or consistent 
in their use of ‘`’ or propositional connectives. Nor were they very careful when they 
explained the classical connectives in terms of semantic ascent. On the other hand, 
Le´sniewski’s criticism does not seem as crippling as he himself (quite uncharitably) took 
it to be: 
. . . the reader will constantly wilt under the weight of this medley of inter­
pretations. The impenetrable chaos which is the actual result of the reader’s 
eﬀort aimed at grasping the sense of the ‘primitive proposition’ ?1.3., could 
be even intensiﬁed, if the reader refers to a series of other comments contained 
in the work of Whitehead and Russell and connected with the comments on 
the mentioned problems, which do not harmonize with the authors’ comments 
already quoted or even with one another. (Le´sniewski 1927: 194) 

92 
Still, Le´sniewski’s suggestion was that one should ignore the assertion sign (not be­
cause it is superﬂuous but rather because it muddles things) and avoid any kind of 
treatment of complex formulas which takes them to be saying something about their 
constitutive formulas. 
3.5 Axiomatic formulations 
What follows is a simpliﬁcation of the original account. The main issue here is that 
Le´sniewski was a nominalist, and his directives were not directives describing some ab­
stract entity (say, a set of theorems), but rather a description of how a system seen 
as a bunch of inscriptions is to be developed. For, indeed, his view on logical systems 
was quite dynamic: theorems are written expressions, and the system physically grows 
or develops as new theorems and proofs are being written down. This speciﬁc feature 
was mirrored in Le´sniewski’s explanation of his systems: he was really concerned with 
speaking of his system as if it were a set of inscriptions. So, for instance, depending on 
in what stage of the development the system is, some expressions may be meaningful or 
meaningless according to whether their deﬁnitions have been already written, etc. For 
the sake of simplicity of our description I will not follow this style, thus presenting a 
somewhat ‘Platonized’ version of Protothetic. 
A few words about notational conventions. Variables p, q, r are propositional variables 
within the system, variables f, g are variables of categories diﬀerent than s (the context 
will determine their category). φ, ψ, χ are meta–linguistic variables standing for formu­
las, ζ (possibly with subscripts) are schematic meta–variables representing variables of 
categories diﬀerent from the category of sentences and τ is a schematic letter standing 
for a constant (i.e. a propositional constant or a functor of certain category other than 
the category of sentences).15 
15At one point, (ζ) is a name of a rule, but the context should make it clear. 

93 
System S. As discussed in (Le´sniewski 1929a: 423–438) it had two axioms: 
((p ≡ r) ≡ (q ≡ p)) ≡ (r ≡ q) 
(p ≡ (q ≡ r)) ≡ ((p ≡ q) ≡ r) 
and two rules: 
— From ψ ≡ χ and ψ infer χ (called detachment for equivalence). 
— From ψ ≡ χ and φ infer φ(χ) which is a result of replacing some of occurrences of 
ψ in φ by χ (called substitution for equivalence). 
The system is not functionally complete, but it has an interesting feature: for any 
expression φ in the language of this system (that is, correctly constructed from propo­
sitional variables and the equivalence symbol) this expression is a theorem of S iﬀ any 
propositional variable in φ occurs an even number of times.16 
System S1. This system (Le´sniewski 1929a: 438–450) admitted quantiﬁers binding 
propositional variables and ﬁrst-order functors. There were three axioms: 
∀p,q,r(((p ≡ r) ≡ (q ≡ p)) ≡ (r ≡ q)) 
∀p,q,r((p ≡ (q ≡ r)) ≡ ((p ≡ q) ≡ r)) 
∀g,p(∀f (g(p, p) ≡∀r((f(r, r) ≡ g(p, p)) ≡∀r(f(r, r) ≡ 
≡ g(p ≡∀qq, p))) ≡∀qg(p, p))) 
Tarski (1923) has shown that the system is functionally complete. 
It is common in the literature to call quantiﬁers binding propositional variables only 
ﬁrst-order protothetical quantiﬁers, and to call other quantiﬁers higher order prototheti­
cal quantiﬁers. Note that ﬁrst order propositional quantiﬁcation alone would not suﬃce 
16This seems to be the ﬁrst form of the so-called Le´sniewski-Mihailescu theorem. 

94 
to deﬁne all truth–functional connectives using equivalence as the sole primitive connec­
tive (see Surma 1977 for details). It only allows one to deﬁne two propositional constants: 
false (∀pp) and true (∀p(p ≡ p)), and four functions of one argument: assertion (p), nega­
tion (∀pp ≡ p), the verum of p (p ≡ p) and the falsum of p (∀p(p ≡ p)) but this does not 
constitute functional completeness. The deﬁnition of conjunction requires higher-order 
protothetical quantiﬁcation: 
p ∧ q ≡∀f (p ≡ (f(p) ≡ f(q))) 
(3.9) 
Having deﬁned negation (using ﬁrst–order quantiﬁcation) and conjunction (using higher– 
order quantiﬁcation) this way Tarski was able to obtain the functional completeness 
result. 
S1 had six rules: 
(α) detachment for equivalence. 
(β) substitution: this works almost like universal quantiﬁer elimination in natural 
deduction systems. From ∀ζ φ it allows one to infer the expression resulting from sub­
stituting in φ all occurrences of ζ (free in φ) by a simple expression of the language 
(constants, variables) given that no substituted variable becomes bound. Strictly speak­
ing, no complex expression can be used in a substitution. However, one can get the same 
result as if complex expressions could be used, introducing simple expressions ‘equiva­
lent’ to the complex expression by means of deﬁnitions. This rule applies to propositional 
variables and functor variables as well. 
(γ) Let all free variables in χ be p1, . . . , pk, and let all free variables in ψ be pk+1, . . . , pm. 
From ∀p1,...,pm (χ ≡ ψ) infer ∀p1,...,pk χ ≡∀pk+1,...,pm ψ. An analogous rule pertains to uni­
versal quantiﬁers binding functor variables (distribution). 
(δ) τ ≡ χ is a deﬁnition of a newly introduced propositional constant τ iﬀ χ is a 
well-formed formula with no free variables (absolute deﬁnitions). 

95 
() ∀ζ1,...,ζn (τ(ζ1, . . . , ζn) ≡ χ(ζ1, . . . , ζn)) is a deﬁnition of a newly introduced n-place 
functor τ, iﬀ ζ1, . . . , ζn are the only free variables in the wﬀ χ(ζ1, . . . , ζn) (non-primitive 
expressions cannot occur in wﬀs unless they are earlier deﬁned). 
(ζ) a rule concerning quantiﬁers. Originally it was formulated for a language with 
implication as the primitive symbol. No clear description of this rule exists in the sources. 
It was supposed to move universal quantiﬁer from the front of a conditional to the front 
of the consequent, given that no variable bound by this quantiﬁer occurred as free in the 
antecedent. What we know is that Tarski in 1922 proved it to be dependent on the other 
rules and as such redundant (so we do not need it anyway). (Srzednicki and Stachniak 
1988: 22–23)) 
System S2. Described in (Le´sniewski 1929a: 451–53), it is an extension of S1 by a rule 
called (η). This rule originates from an idea due to  Lukasiewicz, which goes as follows: 
Two propositional constants are introduced: 
0 ≡∀qq 
1 ≡ (∀qq ≡∀qq) 
Whenever for a formula φ all possible substitutions of propositional variables in φ by 
0’s and 1’s are sentences provable in S1, the sentence constructed from the formula φ 
by binding all free propositional variables in φ by universal quantiﬁers preceding φ is a 
theorem. Le´sniewski introduced an analogous rule for semantic categories other than s. 
So, simplifying, for any semantic category σ there is a speciﬁc set of constants S 
(called veriﬁers) such that for any formula φ if all results of substitution of free variables 
of the category σ by elements of S are provable in S1, then the formula φ preceded by 
universal quantiﬁers binding these variables is a theorem of S2. 
For instance, for the semantic category s, the propositional constants would have 
s
been 1 and 0, as deﬁned above. For the category s,s , they are all 16 truth-functional 

96 
connectives. In general the veriﬁers of a given semantic category are all (up to logical 
equivalence) diﬀerently deﬁned constants of that category. Le´sniewski provided a detailed 
explanation of what a veriﬁer is. For the purpose of this historic survey, the intuitive 
content of the rule should be suﬃcient. 
Le´sniewski did not want rule η to allow the introduction of universal quantiﬁers 
binding propositional variables. So, in his formulation, the rule (i) describes the form of 
all veriﬁers of a certain category other than s (basically, the deﬁnientes of all extensional 
constants of that category respectively) and (ii) allows one to introduce ∀ζ φ(ζ) if all 
instances resulting from the substitution of veriﬁers of the same category as ζ for ζ in 
φ(ζ) have been proven. A likely reason why this rule excluded the case of a universal 
quantiﬁer binding a propositional variable is that this would make the rule partially 
redundant. Other rules handle quantiﬁers binding propositional variables suﬃciently, 
i.e. if an expression can be proved by applying η to propositional variables, then it was 
already provable in S1. 
System S3. (Le´sniewski 1929a: 454–464) arose from the fact that the formulation 
of (η) requires quite a complicated technical apparatus (determining what the set of 
relevant constants for each semantic category is). Thus, Le´sniewski proposed a rule of 
extensionality, called (η?) which turns out to do the same job as (η). The rule says that 
for any one-place functor of at least second order the corresponding law of extensionality 
(to be deﬁned below) is a theorem. 
To formulate the law of extensionality for higher-order functors we have to deﬁne 
their equivalence ﬁrst. 
Deﬁnition 4 (Higher-order Equivalence). The ﬁrst-order equivalence of sentential con­

The last axiom is quite interesting. Let us see what it says and why it is valid. It 
states that for any two–place connective g, if g(p, q) is true and g(p →∀qq, p) (that 
is, g( p, p), because ∀qq is disprovable) is true, then also g(q, p) is true. Why? Well, 
¬
Protothetic is a two–valued extensional logic. Hence, under any valuation q has to be 
either (materially) equivalent to p or to p. If it has the same truth–value as p, then

we can substitute q for 
p in g( p, p) and hence g(q, p). If, on the other hand, q has
¬

the same truth value as p, we can substitute p for q in g(p, q) and so we get g(p, p). But 
then, we also can substitute q for p and we get g(q, p) as well.

Later (1922–1923), this system was simpliﬁed. The ﬁrst important simpliﬁcation is 
due to Tarski (see Le´sniewski 1929a: 459), who (i) suggested writing deﬁnitions in the 
form of two separate implications, and (ii) showed that it will suﬃce to use (3.10) and 
the following axiom of veriﬁcation instead of all the above axioms: 
∀f,p,q,r(f(r, p)

System S5 
is the ﬁnal form of Protothetic. It is a slight modiﬁcation of the rule of 
deﬁnition: deﬁnienda are placed on the right side of the equivalence and not on the left) 
and uses the same rules as S3. The diﬀerence is that it makes proofs shorter, because 
it allows one to apply universal quantiﬁer distribution and detachment to deﬁnitions 
without employing the theorem stating that equivalence is symmetric. 
3.6 (Annotated) further readings 
Historically speaking, Protothetic was preceded by certain suggestions concerning propo­
sitional quantiﬁcation. Russell (1906) suggested introducing quantiﬁers binding the 
propositional variables, but he did not use this idea.  Lukasiewicz (1929) developed a 
calculus where quantiﬁers binding propositional variables play an important part (see 
also  Lukasiewicz and Tarski 1930), but it seems that the generality that Protothetic can 
aﬀord was Le´sniewski’s idea. The very ﬁrst paper related to Protothetic which employs 
quantiﬁcation not only over propositional variables but also over variables representing 
connectives is Tarski (1923, reprinted in Srzednicki and Stachniak 1998), where it was 
shown that when this sort of generality is admitted, conjunction can be deﬁned in terms of 
equivalence as the only connective. Tarski’s results on functional completeness of Proto­
thetic were extended by Soboci´nski (1949a, reprinted in Srzednicki and Stachniak 1998), 
who generalized Tarski’s considerations concerning forms of deﬁnitions in Protothetic. 

100 
Soboci´nski’s paper is also interesting for historical reasons — in the introduction he de­
scribes the fate of Collectanea Logica, the Polish journal in which (Le´sniewski 1938a,b) 
were to appear. The outbreak of war made its publication impossible. 
The project of ﬁnding the shortest possible axiomatization of Protothetic, already 
initiated by Le´sniewski, was continued not only by him but also by some of his students 
for quite a few years. 
Soboci´nski (1954b, 1960, 1961) are good surveys of the results. The latter paper also 
contains an interesting metatheorem which states a suﬃcient condition for an axiomati­
zation of Protothetic (although no proof is given). Soboci´nski also gave another, slightly 
more complicated version of suﬃcient conditions for completeness, also without a proof 
(with some explanation, though). Le Blanc (1985) provides another, shorter version of 
the axiomatization. 
One of the best introductions to Protothetic so far is S lupecki (1953). The formula­
tions that S lupecki focuses on are mainly conditional. His explanations of the rules of 
inference are somewhat vague, but he provides many examples of simple proofs which 
allow the reader to get an idea as to what rules he is using. In one aspect his rules dif­
fer from those of Le´sniewski: he allows for substitution of complex expressions in place 
of variables of appropriate semantic categories, which was forbidden in the original for­
mulation — this move, however, is conservative. S lupecki does not prove this in whole 
generality, but gives a convincing argument. A nice feature of S lupecki’s presentation is 
that he starts with relatively simple systems and goes on to construct full Protothetic 
by gradual strengthening. He ﬁrst gives propositional calculus with quantiﬁers binding 
propositional connectives only. Another nice feature of the conditional formulation is 
that ﬁrst-order quantiﬁcation is suﬃcient for deﬁning negation, and the conditional and 
negation provide a functionally complete basis. The disadvantage is that in the oﬃcial 

101 
notation deﬁnitions have the form of two separate conditionals.19 So, for example, the 
deﬁnition of negation is: 
∀p(¬p → (p →∀pp)) 
(3.14) 
∀p((p →∀pp) →¬p) 
(3.15) 
Next, he discussed some interesting theorems that can be formulated in elementary Pro­
tothetic, which he also proved to be provably equivalent in elementary Protothetic (for 
one-place function symbols, at least, but a generalization of his results is to be found by 
Chikawa (1967)). He proved that for any theorem of the classical propositional calcu­
lus the formula constructed by binding all the free variables in the formula by universal 
quantiﬁers preceding the whole formula is a theorem of elementary Protothetic. The 
ﬁrst extension of elementary Protothetic to the full-blown Protothetic that he discusses 
employs the rule of veriﬁcation. Basically, it consists in deﬁning what the appropriate 
substitution class for universal quantiﬁers of any semantic category is and saying that 
if all substitutions of a universal claim are provable, the universal claim itself is prov­
able. This formulation has the advantage that the completeness proof is relatively simple. 
Another extension of elementary Protothetic consists in introducing the so-called rule of 
extensionality, which is also discussed by S lupecki. He states the equivalence of those two 
systems, although his meta-logical proofs are very sketchy. A sketch of a completeness 
proof is included, but it is not obviously convincing. 
Rickey (1972, (1973), provides a really good insight into the nature of Le´sniewski’s 
terminological explanations, with special attention to how inscriptional description of the 
rules of Protothetic is to be understood. 
An interesting book (in French), intended as an introduction to Protothetic, is 
(Mi´eville 2001). This book certainly does a better job than (Luschei 1962), the pre­
19This formulation resembles the conditional formulation of Protothetic given by Le´sniewski. The main 
diﬀerence is that in Le´sniewski’s conditional formulation deﬁnitions had the form of a single, although 
slightly complex, formula. 

102 
sentation is quite relaxed, though. 
Le´sniewski’s idiosyncratic wheel-and-spoke notation is interesting for at least two rea­
sons: (i) symbols for propositional one- and two-place connectives encode their truth-
tables with their own shape, (ii) geometric relations between diﬀerent symbols correspond 
to various logical relations between the functions that they express. Recently it has been 
shown that there is a simple algorithm that extends this method to arbitrary ﬁrst-order 
classical connectives (Urbaniak 2006b). 
A comprehensive monograph on Protothetic has been published recently 
(L´opez-Escobar and Miraglia 2002), but it is mostly concerned with constructing in­
tuitionist Protothetic. 
Another group of formulations of Protothetic, hinted at by Le´sniewski, is called com­
putative Protothetic and was the subject of a PhD thesis at the University of Manchester 
(Le Blanc 1991). In the system there are signs for assertion and rejection, and rules per­
tain to which formulas are asserted or rejected on the basis of what has been previously 
asserted or rejected. The oldest system of computative Protothetic has as its axiom: 
A : Λ 
Λ 
(3.16)
→ 
where Λ is the falsum constant and A means assertion, and contained nine rules of 
inference. Le Blanc describes sixteen equivalent formulations, based on ten diﬀerent 
combinations of primitive terms. He reconstructs those systems and provides a detailed 
description of their rules. Also, arguments for consistency, completeness and equivalence 
with the standard systems of Protothetic are given. However, these are not detailed and 
complete proofs. 
An interesting research direction is many-valued Protothetics. They have been studied 
(and a completeness result has been given) by Scharle (1971). It turns out that ﬁnitely-
valued Protothetic can be axiomatized with the following property: for every closed wﬀ 

103 
φ one of Ji(φ) is provable, where Ji is a function that takes one of the designated values 
only if its argument has value i. The full proof of this fact can be given using a technique 
developed in Surma (1976) which uses ﬁnitely generated trees. Many-valued Protothetics 
have been the subject of a PhD dissertation at the University of Manchester, supervised 
by Lejewski (Watanabe 1973). As Watanabe has shown, the change in the number of 
admissible values results in the possibility of introducing diﬀerent general quantiﬁers of 
one and the same semantic category, corresponding to diﬀerent conjunctions one can 
introduce in a many-valued system. 
There is a connection between Protothetic and Henkin’s theory of propositional 
types (Henkin 1950, 1963), which was an improvement of the system of Church (1940). 
(Andrews (1963) constitutes an improvement of Henkin’s theory). Minor details aside, 
Henkin’s theory of propositional types can be thought of as a version of Protothetic. 
Grzegorczyk (1964) shows that every one of Henkin’s equality symbols is deﬁnable by 
mean of relational abstraction and the equality symbol for the usual material equivalence 
(although still the set of axioms is denumerable). Meredith (1951) provides also a similar 
system, commented on by Arai and Tanaka (1966). 
Propositional calculi become more complex when we admit non-extensional operators. 
One of the intuitive steps is to introduce an identity operator which expresses something 
like ‘expresses the same proposition’ or ‘states the same fact’. If the criteria of identity 
are more ﬁne-grained than in Frege’s account (according to which the denotation of any 
two sentences having the same truth value is the same), we get non-extensional calculi. 
Zero-order calculi with additional axioms and rules for identity, called Sentential Calculi 
with Identity (SCI), have been extensively studied by Suszko (1966, 1967, 1971f,e,d,b,a,c, 
1972b,a, 1973, 1975, 1977) and developed by Omy la (1994). Apparently quite indepen­
dently, following a suggestion of Prior (1957), Cresswell constructed a second-order the­
ory of intensional identity. Besides the diﬀerence of level, in Suszko’s system identity is 

104 
a primitive symbol introduced axiomatically, whereas in Cresswell’s system identity is 
deﬁned by: 
p = q ≡∀f (f(p) ≡ f(q)) 
(3.17) 
where functorial variables are allowed to range over non-extensional functors as well. 
The question of the relation between Cresswell’s and Suszko’s systems has not been fully 
explored. Also, Suszko at one point suggests that Protothetic would have been much more 
interesting if it had admitted the intensional identity. No such extension of Protothetic 
has been constructed so far. Another fascinating issue seems to be the relation between 
intensional identity and modalities. In his papers Suszko also shows how in some systems 
with identity as the only primitive non-extensional system it is possible to obtain various 
modal calculi. 

Chapter 4 
Le´sniewski’s Ontology 
4.1 Ontology — basic intuitions 
The ﬁrst remark on Ontology, a logical system developed by Le´sniewski, dates back to his 
introduction to “On the Foundations of Mathematics”. It is a second layer of his systems. 
The ﬁrst layer, Protothetic, consisted of a logic concerned mostly with propositional 
variables. The second layer extends this by introducing a category of names (and other 
derivative categories) and a predication operator: ε. Without describing the system he 
says that Ontology. . . 
. . . forms a certain kind of modernized ‘traditional logic’, and which in content 
and ‘power’ most nearly approaches the Schr¨oder ‘Klassenkalk¨ul’, considered 
as including the theory of ‘individuals’. (1927: 176) 
The ﬁrst axiomatization of Ontology1 Le´sniewski claims to have formulated while 
teaching a course ‘Exercises in the domain of the Cantorian Theory of Sets’ at Warsaw 
University in 1919/1920 (1931a: 367). 
The ﬁrst oﬃcial presentation of Ontology was Le´sniewski’s lecture in Warsaw on 
January 10, 1921, titled ‘On the principles of Ontology’ at the scientiﬁc session of the 
Polish Association for Psychology (1931a: 369). 
Ontology, a theory more general that Protothetic, is mostly concerned with predica­
tion, but it diﬀers from the classical ﬁrst order logic in a few respects. 
1All other formulations of Ontology consist in replacing the axiom of Ontology with another, provably 
equivalent axiom or axioms. 
105 

106 
First of all, it introduces terms, but it makes no distinction between names and 
predicates. In this respect, it follows the traditional syllogistic rather than the predicate 
calculus. That is, it assumes only one category of names, no matter whether they are 
empty, singular or common. So, a wide understanding of the word name is accepted 
here. A name is any expression that may take the place of either M or N in a sentence 
of the form: M is N. Or, in other words, all countable categorematic (as opposed 
to syncategorematic) expressions that are not sentences are taken to be names. Some 
examples of names in this wide understanding are: (a) cat, Socrates, brown, the bald guy 
in the corner. 
Le´sniewski’s Ontology can be viewed as an extension of name calculus in the wide 
sense of the word ‘name’ mentioned above. That is, the speciﬁc variables (a, b, c, d, . . .) 
that are used in Ontology are name variables.2 It can be thought of as an extension 
because it also allows for variables of higher categories which involve categories of names. 
One of the possible ways to think of name variables is that they correspond to possible 
countable noun phrases. Another way, slightly more in Le´sniewski’s spirit, is that they 
represent ways in which a name can refer. 
Referring to nothing at all is also a way in which a name can refer. In fact, names that 
are represented by variables may be empty names (Unicorn), singular terms (Socrates), 
or general terms ((all) cats). Thus, by including the case when a name does not refer, 
Le´sniewski’s logic, in a sense, is one of the very ﬁrst formalized free logics. 
A second respect in which Ontology diﬀers from standard ﬁrst–order logic is that it 
represents predication in a diﬀerent way than ﬁrst order languages. Besides quantiﬁers3 
and classical propositional connectives, Ontology has another primitive symbol, the ep­
silon connective ε (read as ‘is’ or ‘is one of’). An expression of the form a ε b is read as ‘a is 
2It does not mean that no variables of other categories are not used. It just means that the intro­
duction of name variables is one of the most characteristic feature of Ontology. 
3Historically speaking, Le´sniewski used only the universal quantiﬁer. 

107 
b’ (‘a is one of b’s’) and it is true if and only if a is taken to be a singular term that names 
an object which is among the objects named by b. So, the following are true: Socrates 
ε philosopher, The book on my desk ε Kleene’s ‘Introduction to Metamathematics’, and 
the following turn out to be false (although still well-formed): Unicorn ε animal, ele­
phant ε animal. Some examples of the rendering of simple quantiﬁed statements: ‘some 
philosophers are mad’ (‘some b is c’) has the form ∃a(a ε b ∧ a ε c) and ‘all mad people 
think they are philosophers’ (‘all b are c’) becomes something like ∀a(a ε b → a ε c).4 
Third, in Ontology quantiﬁers do not carry ontological commitment. It is the use of 
ε by which existence is stated. So, existence is deﬁned: 
∀a(ex(a) ≡∃bb ε a) 
(4.1) 
and it is provable in Ontology that for some a, a does not exist: 
∃a¬ex(a) 
(4.2) 
This and similar facts raised a fairly complex debate about the interpretation of 
Le´sniewski’s quantiﬁers and the meaning of variables in his systems. I list related liter­
ature in section 4.7, and I discuss the problem of interpretation of quantiﬁers in more 
depth in chapter 7. 
4.2 The language of Ontology 
The notion of semantic category as applied to Ontology is the notion that we already 
know from section 3.2. What is new in comparison to Protothetic, expressions of the 
category n of names are introduced, and expressions of categories that can be built from

108 
s’s and n’s are allowed, pretty much as in Protothetic we allowed categories built from 
s’s only (see deﬁnition 1 on page 75). 
All formulas of type a ε b are of category s. All terms are of category n. Any expression 
which with n arguments of categories σ1, . . . , σn yields an expression of the category σ is

The language of Ontology arises from a generalization parallel to that of Protothetic: 
for any semantic category of Ontology, constants can be deﬁned, and variables together 
with quantiﬁers binding those variables can be introduced.

4.3 The axiomatic system 
Ontology, in its version from 1920 as described by Le´sniewski in his (1931a: 364-369), 
has a single axiom:9 
∀a,b[a ε b ≡∃c(c ε a) ∧∀c,d(c ε a ∧ d ε a 
c ε d) ∧∀c(c ε a 
c ε b)] 
(4.3)
→ 
→ 
This axiom says that for any a and b, a is b iﬀ (i) for some c, c is a, (ii) for any c and d, 
if c is a and d is a, then c is d, and (iii) for any c, if c is a, then c is also b. 
The interpretation of ε10 is that a ε b is true if and only if there is exactly one object 
which is a, and this object is among the objects which are b (this includes the case where 
b names exactly one object). Accordingly, (i) is supposed to guarantee the non–emptiness 
of a, (ii) is intended to express the requirement that a names at most one object, and 
(iii) is meant to state the inclusion between a and b (‘any object which is a, is b’). 
In a somewhat modernized version (equivalent to the original) one can use standard 
rules for quantiﬁers and apply propositional logic just as is it done in predicate logic.11 
That is, the quantiﬁer elimination and introduction procedures are just an extension of 
classical rules to variables of higher categories. Besides there are a few speciﬁc rules, 
9However, it has certain axiomatic rules that allow for introduction of an inﬁnite number of theorems 
without any premises. 
10Which is in no way determined by the axiomatic basis of Ontology, see (Urbaniak 2006c) for details. 
11Originally, the rules for quantiﬁers and propositional inference were a trivial extension (to quan­
tiﬁcation over variables of diﬀerent categories) of the rules employed in Protothetic, see chapter 3 for 
details. 

110 
especially connected with deﬁnitions and introduction of variables of diﬀerent semantic 
categories. 
Deﬁnitions of constant name expressions have the form: 
∀a[a ε Δ ≡ a ε a ∧ φ] 
(4.4) 
where Δ is the deﬁned term and φ is a formula with a as its only free variable.

It is common to say that Ontology has a single axiom. In a way, this is true. The 
reader may however notice that rules of deﬁnition and the rule of extensionality can 
easily be taken to be axiom schemata. On this reading, it is more appropriate to say 
that Ontology has an inﬁnite number of axioms, one of which is intended to introduce 
ε , the speciﬁc functor of Ontology.

∀f,a[R(f)(a) ≡∃bf(b, a)] 
(4.27) 
s
For any n,n expression, its restriction to speciﬁc domains and ranges is introduced by: 
∀f,g,h,a,b[Restr(f, g, h)(a, b) ≡ f(a, b) ∧ g(a) ∧ h(b)] 
(4.28) 
For any two-place functor we can deﬁne its reﬂexivity, transitivity, symmetry, anti-
symmetry. Thus we can also express the fact that it is a partial or well-ordering. Pretty 
much all the standard notions can be introduced: ﬁrst, least, maximal, greatest element 
of a given relation, l.u.b.’s, g.l.b.’s, chains etc. (w.r.t. a restricted domain or not). 
4.5 Against universals 
As an example of how the language of Ontology may be used to formalize philosophical 
arguments I will discuss now one of Le´sniewski’s arguments against the existence of 
universals. 
Le´sniewski (1927: 199) constructed an argument against the existence of universals, 
very similar to the one from (1913b: 50–53).14 The diﬀerence is that it does not employ 
the notion of property and it does not employ negative properties. In its original version 
it consists of one assumption and six conclusions following subsequently. Even though 
it is not formalized in Ontology, the way it is phrased makes the formalization relatively 
easy. 
Here is Le´sniewski’s original version. Just like his early argument against universals, 
this one starts with a very speciﬁc assumption about abstract objects: 
However, in connection with that passage and with reference to all those 
who, by reason of the meaning they give to expressions of the type ‘general 
14The latter has been already discussed in section 2.8. 

115 
object’15 with respect to objects a, 16 are inclined to state the proposition ‘if 
X is a general object with respect to objects a, X is b and Y is a, then Y is 
b’,17 I wish to state here that this proposition entails the proposition ‘if there 
exist at least two diﬀerent a, then a general object with respect to objects a 
does not exist,’ in accordance with the following schema: 
(1) If X is a general object with respect to objects a, X is b, [and] also Y is 
a, then Y is b. (assumption) 
(2) If X is a general object with respect to objects a, X is diﬀerent from Z, 
and Z is a, then Z is diﬀerent from Z. 
(3) If X is a general object with respect to objects a, X is identical with Z 
and Y is a, then Y is identical with Z. 
From (2) it follows that: 
(4) If X is a general object with respect to objects a, and Z is a, then X is 
identical with Z. 
(5) If X is a general object with respect to objects a, Z is a and Y is a, then 
(X is a general object with respect to objects a, X is identical with Z, and 
Y is a). 
From (5) and (3) it follows that: 
(6) If X is a general object with respect to objects a, Z is a and Y is a, then 
Y is identical with Z. 
From (6) however it follows that if there exist at least two diﬀerent a, then a 
15By ‘general object’ Le´sniewski understood universals. 
16For instance, in this sense (at ﬁrst approximation), redness would be a general object with respect 
to all red objects. 
17Le´sniewski informally used capital letters as name variables in formulas if the truth of a formula 
required that only one object be assigned to a variable. So, instead of a, if the truth of a formula requires 
that only one object is a, Le´sniewski would use a capital letter, like X, Y or Z. For the sake of uniformity 
I do not use capital letters as name variables in the formalism. 

116 
general object with respect to objects a does not exist. 
Formalized reconstructions of the full argument have been given by Waragai (1981) 
and Gryganiec (2000). I will give a sketch of a simpliﬁed proof which starts with the 
formalization of Le´sniewski’s assumption and ends with his ﬁnal conclusion. 
The assumption is: 
If X is a general object with respect to objects a, X is b, also Y is a, then Y 
is b. (1927: 199) 
The intuition here is pretty much the same as the one behind Le´sniewski’s ﬁrst argu­
ment. We start with a bunch of objects, all named by a name a, and put a condition for 
an object, say c, to be a general object with respect to those objects. Namely, whatever 
is predicable of this general object should be predicable about any of the objects among 
a’s. 
If we represent is using the epsilon, and treat ‘Gen’ as a one-place functor that with 
one name argument, say a, constructs a name Gen(a) (‘The general objects with respect 
to a’s’), this assumption may be formalized by: 
∀a,b,c,d[c ε Gen(a)

The bottom line here is that if something is the general object with respect to a’s, there 
is at most one a, so there can be no object which is general with respect to more than 
one object.

Le´sniewski’s comment on the argument is: 

118 
I regard my treatment as the result of a careful formulation of theoretical 
tendencies involved, more or less explicitly, in the argumentation of opponents 
of the diﬀerent kinds of ‘universals’ in various phases of their ‘disputes’ about 
them. If one takes the position that this assertion is a banal one, I would cite 
in defence the circumstance that exponents of ‘philosophy’ defend, regrettably 
often, positions at variance with banal assertions. (1927: 199) 
4.6 Le´sniewski’s original style of presentation 
The question arises: to what extent does my presentation diverge from that of Le´sniewski? 
To address this concern, I will discuss two aspects in which a comparison can be made. 
The language used within the system, in which theorems of various systems are formu­
lated is one of them. Another is the meta–logical approach: I mentioned that I Platonized 
Le´sniewski’s systems for the sake of presentation. What does that mean? 
In his early works on Mereology Le´sniewski just lists the axioms. For instance, the 
ﬁrst formulations of axiom I and of deﬁnition I of Mereology look as follows: 
Axiom I. If object P is a part of object P1, then object P1 is not a part of 
object P . (Le´sniewski 1916: 131) 
Definition I. I use the expression ‘ingredient of object P’ to denote the 
same object P and every part of that object. (Le´sniewski 1916: 132) 
Although at that time he did not give any derivation rules, he nevertheless gave proofs 
for his theorems. For instance, his proof of Theorem XI. (If P1 is an ingredient of object 
P , then P1 is an element of object P ) is: 
Proof: We assume, that: 
(1) P1 is an ingredient of object P . 

119 
About object P we may say — in accordance with Theorem VIII — that 
(2) P is the class of ingredients of object P . 
Using the expressions ‘x’ with the meaning of the expression ‘ingredient of 
object P ’, we infer from assertions (2) and (1) that 
(3) P is the class of objects x. 
(4) P1 is x. 
From assertions (3) and (4) it follows in accordance with Deﬁnition IV that 
(5) P1 is an element of object P . 
Thus, assuming assertion (1), we arrive at assertion (5). It follows from this 
that if P1 is an ingredient of P , then P1 is an element of object P , which was 
to be proved. (Le´sniewski 1916: 139) 
Le´sniewski employs the same style of presentation in all his papers on Mereology, up 
to Chapter X of his ‘On the Foundations of Mathematics’. Chapter XI introduces his 
ε and describes the axiom of Ontology. There (Le´sniewski 1931a: 369), he employs a 
formalism based on that of Principia: 
(A, a)::A ε a.≡ ∴(∃B).B ε A ∴ (B, C): B ε A . C ε A. ⊃. B ε C ∴ (B) : B ε A . ⊃ 
. B ε a 
He uses the same notation when he describes his Protothetic in 1929a. For instance, 
his formulation of two axioms of the simplest subsystem of Protothetic that he discusses 
is (p. 423): 
A1.
p ≡ r. ≡ .q ≡ p: ≡: r ≡ q 
A2.
p ≡ .q ≡ r : ≡: p ≡ q. ≡ r 
In the same manner he states and proves theorems of this system. When he describes 

120 
his S1 (Le´sniewski 1929a: 441) he switches to his own notation18 and usually keeps using 
it when proving theorems of Protothetic in subsequent publications (he did not publish 
proofs of any theorems of Ontology or Mereology after he introduced his notation). 
Another question is: how did Le´sniewski describe his rules of inference? On one 
hand, he brieﬂy described his rules informally, but this description is not very precise. 
For instance, when he introduces rules of inference for S1 he starts by saying: 
Using the following six directives, I obtained new propositions in S1 on the 
basis of propositions already belonging to the system (for the present I limit 
myself in this section to a quite general characterization of these directives): 
(α) the ‘detachment’ directive – as in S. 
(β) the ‘substitution directive’? 
(γ) the ‘distribution of quantiﬁers’ directive by which, in case some thesis T 
compounded of a universal quantiﬁer Q and an equivalence A standing under 
the quantiﬁer already belonging to the system, it is permissible to add to 
the system a new thesis which is formed from thesis T through ‘transferring’ 
— by an exactly determined (but in practice, no doubt, somewhat artiﬁcial) 
way — all or only some variables contained in the quantiﬁer into the quan­
tiﬁers in front of the left and in front of the right side of the equivalence 
A. . . (Le´sniewski 1929a: 442) 
The star sends the reader to a footnote, where Le´sniewski says: 
Cf. below in paragraph 11, TE XLVII and TE XLVIII and further below in 
the same (paragraph 11 point 4) of the direction concerning the method of 
construction of S5. 
What are the TE’s that he mentions? Because Le´sniewski was a nominalist, he 
18I discussed his notation in section 3.3. 

121 
believed that a description of a logical system, instead of being a description of an 
abstract set of formulas, is rather a set of rules according to which one can develop 
a system of inscriptions which would count as theorems of his system. Rules which 
determine how a particular, token–based version of a system can be written down are 
called terminological explanations. 19 They are the way that Le´sniewski ultimately 
chose to describe his systems. 
Terminological explanations deﬁnitively diﬀer from what we nowadays would consider 
a description of a logical system. First, there are no two separate sets of rules, those 
of formation and those of derivation, but only one set of general rules whose ultimate 
purpose is to determine what inscriptions can be added to a system when one is developing 
his own group of inscriptions. There are, overall, 106 rules which describe notions required 
for his explanation of theoremhood of Protothetic and Ontology. The language in which 
they are formulated is ﬁlled with Le´sniewski’s idiosyncratic terms and abbreviations, 
which makes it quite hermetic, even though the formulation is extremely meticulous.20 
The idea that there is no single axiomatic system of Ontology, but rather a general set 
of rules which describe how one should proceed when one wants to write down his own 
‘copy’ of inscriptions that would count as theorems of Ontology resulted also in a quite 
speciﬁc view of an axiomatic system. 
Instead of postulating that there exists a set of well–formed formulas which just 
needs to be deﬁned, Le´sniewski rather insists that there is no unique system of Ontology. 
Rather, there are instructions as to how to produce certain inscriptions that would count 
as theorems of Ontology (so, strictly speaking, it is improper to speak of the system of 
Ontology or the system of Protothetic). A side–eﬀect of this approach is that certain 
notions are relative to the stage of development of an actual system of Ontology. For 
instance, a notion of a formula is relative to a stage of a development of the formal 
19Le´sniewski used the term ‘Terminologische Erkl¨arungen’. 
20His deﬁnitions do not even include the notion of a well–formed formula. 

122 
system. Also, for instance, one is allowed to write down formulas that follow from certain 
deﬁnitions after writing down those deﬁnitions. 
A terminological explanation21 usually explains a technical term that Le´sniewski uses 
to describe expressions that occur in his systems. They are also formulated in terms 
of the shape and position of symbols. Let us take a closer look at how he develops his 
terminological explanations. 
In Le´sniewski’s notation the ﬁrst axiom of Protothetic is:22 
A1 bpqrcd d( d( d(pr) d(qp)) d(rq))e 
Let us now assume A1 names exactly this token. 
The underlying language of Le´sniewski’s meta–logic is the language of Ontology. So 
the basic atomic proposition is of the type a ε b. Then we have the expression Id(a), 
‘same object as a’, which can be deﬁned by:23 
∀a,b(a ε Id(b) ≡ a ε b ∧ b ε a) 
We also have term negation, ∼ a: 
∀a,b(a ε ∼ b ≡ a ε a ∧¬a ε b) 
And for each k we have deﬁned the term union and intersection:24 
∀a,a1,a2,...,ak (a ε a1 ∩ a2 ∩· · · ∩ ak ≡ a ε a1 ∧ a ε a2 ∧· · · ∧ a ε ak) 
21Terminological explanations describing Protothetic are to be found in (Le´sniewski 1929a: 469-488), 
those describing Ontology are given in (Le´sniewski 1930b), and certain TE’s concerned with deﬁnitions 
have been published in (Le´sniewski 1931b). 
22Except I do not bother to vary the size of brackets. Le´sniewski usually followed the rule that the 
larger the scope of a bracket, the larger the bracket itself. 
23Note that saying that a is the same object as b is not the same as saying that a is b. The former 
entails that there is a unique object b, the latter does not. 
24Strictly speaking, there is no single expression of Ontology that deﬁnes the notions of term union 
and intersection which generates the given schemas. For any given k we can formulate a deﬁnition but 
no expression of Ontology deﬁnes those notions for any k in a uniform way along these lines (basically, 
because in Ontology there is no device that would allow us to quantify over numbers in subscripts). 
What we can get, however, is the notion of generalized union and intersection. Granted that φ is of 

\ 
123 
∀a,a1,a2,...,ak (a ε a1 ∪ a2 ∩· · · ∩ ak ≡ a ε a1 ∨ a ε a2 ∨· · · ∨ a ε ak) 
Two more relations: equinumerosity denoted by ‘∞’ and ‘having fewer objects 
than’ denoted by ‘∂’.25 The sentence ‘a∞b’ reads ‘there are as many objects a as there 
are objects b’. The sentence ‘a∂b’ reads ‘there are more objects b than there are objects 
a’. Both kinds of sentences are also true when there are no objects b at all and the 
intuitive truth conditions for a∞b or a∂b are satisﬁed. 
Now a bunch of speciﬁcally meta-linguistic terms. A name constant ‘vrb’ abbreviates 
the word ‘word’. Basically, every ﬁnite sequence on the alphabet of a language which 
is not further divisible into constituent parts which are expressions of that language is a 
word. Some examples of words are: ‘p’, ‘ d’, ‘(’, ‘b’, ‘c’, ‘1ingr’, ‘sbqnt’.26 On the other 
hand expressions like ‘(p)’, ‘p dq’ are not words. No subexpression of a word is a word. 
The word ‘expression’ is abbreviated by ‘expr’. Any ﬁnite sequence of successive 
words is an expression. A general name naming all parentheses is ‘prnt’. A name 
which names all and only left parentheses is ‘prntl’. Note that corners used to express 
quantiﬁcation (‘b’, ‘c’, ‘d’, ‘e’) are not taken to be parentheses. 
We also assume that we can identify matching parentheses. In Le´sniewski’s own 
notation each pair of matching parentheses in a formula has its own shape or size. If 
they diﬀer only in their scope their length diﬀers.27 If they surround an expression of 
a diﬀerent semantic category, they have diﬀerent shapes. Change in length is not a

25The latter for mainly aesthetical is not the symbol that Le´sniewski originally used. 
26Remember that ‘ d’ is Le´sniewski’s symbol for equivalence and expressions ‘ b’, ‘ c’, ‘ d’, ‘ e ’ mark

diﬀerence in shape. Thus, in a sense, we have groups of matching parenthesis-tokens. If 
a is a parenthesis, its matching parenthesis (which is its symmetrical parenthesis) is 
denoted by ‘prntsym(a)’. 
We also are able to recognize equiform expressions. Equiformity is a relation between 
expressions (which are taken to be tokens). Change in size does not impede equiformity. 
Two equiform expressions written in two diﬀerent places are always taken to be numer­
ically diﬀerent expressions. Given an expression a, a name that names all expressions 
equiform to it is abbreviated by ‘cnf (a)’. 
A name that names all subexpressions of an expression a is abbreviated by ‘ingr(a)’. 
The subexpression relation is taken to be reﬂexive.28 Given an expression a, the name 
that names expressions preceding a is ‘prcd(a)’, the name that names all expres­
sions that follow a is ‘scd(a)’, the name which names the ﬁnal word preceding a is 
‘Uprcd(a)’, and the name of the ﬁnal word occurring in a is ‘Uingr(a)0. In parallel, the 
n-th word occurring in a is denoted by ‘ningr(a)’ (e.g. ‘1ingr(a)’, ‘2ingr(a)’ etc.). 
Instead of material equivalence in terminological explanations, Le´sniewski’s employed 
a meta–linguistic “deﬁnability” operator ‘=’, where ‘a ε b=p’ is to be read as ‘I say of an 
object a that it is a b if and only if p’ (Le´sniewski 1929a: 471). For the sake of clarity I 
will use ‘=:’ instead.

This should give us a good insight into how he proceeded without getting us entangled 
in details.

Since from the above formula the meaning of ‘a ε cmpl(b)’ is not immediately obvious, 
consider the following example. Let ‘a’ name the following token: 
d(pq) 
Let us temporarily name the ﬁrst word in a by ‘1’, the second by ‘2’, the third by ‘3’, the 
fourth by ‘4’, the ﬁfth by ‘5’. Also, let us call any substring of a composed of symbols 
n, . . . , k ‘n . . . k’ (e.g. the string of ﬁrst two symbols of a is denoted by ‘12’ and the string 
of two last symbols of a by ‘45’). 
What name can serve as b in this case? By condition 4, b may name only those 
expressions which occur in a (i.e. substrings of a). By condition 2, any word that occurs 
in a has to occur in one of b’s. By condition 3, any two distinct strings denoted by b 
do not share a word. So, usually (if only a has length > 1) b is a common term naming 
mutually exclusive substrings of a such that all words in a are listed when we list all 
words occurring in b’s.

a is a qntf iﬀ:

Terminological Explanation 9. This terminological explanation (as simpliﬁed by 
Adolf Lindenbaum, see (Le´sniewski 1929a: 473)) tells us what a scope of a quantiﬁer is. 
An expression a is a sbqntf iﬀ: 
1. Some b is inside a. 
2. For any b which is either the ﬁrst word of a or a word inside a there are fewer 
tokens of ‘d’ than there are tokens of ‘e’ in the part of a which follows after b. 
3. For any b which is either inside a or the last word of a there are fewer tokens of ‘e’ 
than there are tokens of ‘d’ in the part of a which precede b. 
Basically, this tells us that a scope of a quantiﬁer (i) begins with a ‘d’ (otherwise 
requirement 3 would be violated by, say, the second word in a, which by requirement 
1 has to exist), (ii) ends with a ‘e’ (otherwise requirement 2 would be violated by, say, 
the second last word in a, which exists by requirement 1), and (iii) cannot contain 
mismatched ‘scope-markers’. For the last point contrast ‘dpqdree’ which is a sbqntf with 
‘dpqdre’ where ﬁrst three words violate requirement 2 or with ‘dpqere’ where the last two 
words violate requirement 3. 
∀a[a ε sbqntf =: 
∃bb ε int(a) ∧ 
∀b(b ε 1ingr(a) ∨ b ε int(a) → 
vrb3 ∩ ingr(a) ∩ scd(b) ∂ vrb4 ∩ inr(a) ∩ scd(b)) ∧ 
∀b(b ε int(a) ∨ b ε Uingr(a) → 
vrb4 ∩ ingr(a) ∩ prcd(b) ∂ vrb3 ∩ ingr(a) ∩ prcd(b))]

1. Some b is a quantiﬁer, occurs in a and the ﬁrst word of a is a word in that quantiﬁer. 
2. Some b is a scope of a quantiﬁer, occurs in a and the last word of a occurs in b. 
3. a is the composition of any quantiﬁer and any scope of a quantiﬁer which satisfy 
the ﬁrst two conditions. 
In other words, a qnr1 is constructed by putting a quantiﬁer and a scope of a quantiﬁer 
immediately one after another.

1. b is a word inside the quantiﬁer of c, 
2. a is equiform to b,

4. for any d, e, if d occurs in c, e is a word inside the quantiﬁer of d, a is equiform to

In other words, a ε var(b, c) iﬀ a is a variable inside the scope of the quantiﬁer of c,
