Theorem 5. There is no empty (mereological) set. 
How does this apply to the previous formulations? Read Kl as KlM . What happens 
with the problematic axioms if we take this reading? Consider (6.3). It may be read as 
saying that for any possible name there is the mereological class constituted by objects 
named by this name. This claim is false. As we have seen, if you take any empty name, 
there is no mereological class that corresponds to it. 
Consider (6.4). It is provably false. Take two names: people (who are alive) and 
halves of people (who are alive). The mereological fusion of people is the same as the 
mereological fusion of halves of people, but it is false that whatever is a half of a man is 
a man, or that whatever is a man is a half of a man. Thus we have dealt with the ﬁrst 
two formulations. 
And how about (6.6), (6.8), (6.9), (6.10)? Actually, (6.6) seems true (at least if you 
believe in unrestricted fusions): for any name that names at least one object there is a 
corresponding mereological class. (6.8) also seems to be false, for almost the same reason 
for which we rejected (6.4). For indeed, take the class of people: it is false that whatever 
is a half of a man and is not the class of people itself is a man. On the other hand, (6.9) 
5Note that this deﬁnition is just (6.2). 

183 
seems quite plausible: it just states that the mereological fusion is unique.6 Mereology 
does not provide any basis for disproving (6.10) (nor does it prove it). Actually, the claim 
that there are at least three objects is quite compelling, although it is hard to provide 
any argument to convince someone who already does not believe that this is the case.7 
Thus, if the mereological interpretation of classes is accepted, some of the assumptions 
turn out to be false – actually, they turn out to be quite intuitively so, and the feeling of 
paradox disappears. 
Like practically every solution to a philosophical problem, however, this one generates 
further diﬃculties (as it is quite well known). Mereology was developed by Le´sniewski 
as a system of foundations of mathematics.8 Unfortunately, so far no proof that it can 
perform this task has been given. As Peter Simons points out: 
Le´sniewski’s alternative to set theory is based on the (justiﬁed) assumption: 
If you follow me in what you mean by ‘member’ and ‘set’ you will not get 
into logical trouble. That is true, but the proponents of inconsistent set 
theory were doing more than trying to stay out of trouble (which is why 
they got into it in the ﬁrst place): they were trying to provide a logical 
foundation for mathematics, some of them with a view to showing at least 
part of mathematics to be just logic. Le´sniewski’s weaker system may, or, 
more likely, may not be adequate to that purpose. (Simons 1993: 7) 
6Of course some people reject this view, claiming that diﬀerent mereological fusions are obtained 
from the same constituents depending on the structure of the whole. In response it can just be said that 
they do not have the Le´sniewskian notion of parthood in mind. 
7This does not constitute any practical problem, since a person who would believe that there are at 
most two diﬀerent objects would be quite a rare specimen. 
8In the introduction to his ﬁrst paper on Mereology (1916) he wrote: “ The present work is the ﬁrst 
link in an extended series of works, which I intend to publish in the near or distant future, desiring 
to contribute as much as possible to the justiﬁcation of modern mathematics . . . The arrangement of 
deﬁnitions and truths, which I established in the present work dedicated to the most general problems of 
the theory of sets, has for me, in comparison to other previously known arrangements of deﬁnitions and 
truths (Zermelo, Russell, etc.) this advantage that it eliminates the ‘antinomies’ of the general theory 
of sets without narrowing the original domain of Cantor’s term ‘set’ . . . and on the other hand, it does 
not lead to assertions which are in such startling conﬂict with intuitions of the ‘commonalty’. . . ” 

184 
Indeed, as it is well known, mereological classes in many respects behave diﬀerently 
than it is expected of classes in the classical set theory. For instance: 
• No existence claim of any mereological class is implied by the axioms of Mereology. 
• The empty set does not exist. 
• Since being an element is transitive, there is no obvious iterative hierarchy of sets. 
• If no additional atomistic axioms are introduced, the notion of equinumerosity does 
not seem to apply to mereological classes.9 
• Kuratowski’s deﬁnition of ordered tuples does not work. 
6.3 Distributive classes 
On the other hand, it seems that we have some other notion of class which is far from 
being mereological. There is something compelling about the following sentences: 
(6.22) 
My dog’s leg is not an element of the class of dogs. 
(6.23) 
The class of integers is not an element of the class of integers. 
(6.24) 
The class of the class of elephants is not the same as the class of 
elephants. 
(6.25) 
My dog is an element of the class of dogs. The class of dogs is an 
element of the class of classes of animals. But my dog is not an element of 
the class of classes of animals. 
9Of course, this notion is deﬁned in Ontology, as applying to name variables or constants – the 
question to what extent mathematics can be founded on Ontology lies beyond the scope of this paper. 
The point here is only that mereological sets are indeed much diﬀerent from the sets postulated in, say, 
ZF. 

185 
These are examples of sentences true in the distributive reading.10 But in the collective 
interpretation all above sentences are false. The ﬁrst sentence indicates that the notion 
of distributive element is diﬀerent from parthood: a part of an element of a class does 
not have to be an element of this class. Distributively speaking, the relation of being 
an element is not reﬂexive. It also is not insensitive to iterations. It is not transitive. 
Now, how would Le´sniewski or Soboci´nski account for this sort of intuition?11 Let’s take 
a look at what the latter has to say: 
The expression ‘class(a)’ in the distributive sense is nothing more than a 
ﬁctitious name which replaces the well-known term of classical logic, ‘the 
extension of the objects a’. If one takes the term in this sense, the formula 
‘A ε Kl(a)’ means the same thing as ‘A is an element of the extension of the 
objects a’ or, more brieﬂy, ‘A is a’. In which case, the formula ‘Socrates is 
Kl(white)’ means the same thing as ‘Socrates is an element of the extension of 
white objects’; in other words: ‘Socrates is white’. Thus, the understanding 
10It is important to emphasize that the question I am concerned with is not whether there is a way 
of introducing into the language of Ontology a formal counterpart of the expression ‘the class of’ which 
would mimic correctly Le´sniewski’s understanding of this term (which was quite unusual), but rather 
how the expression ‘the class of’ in the nowadays most common, set-theoretic use can be imitated in 
the language of Le´sniewski’s systems. Thus, even if Le´sniewski would not share the intuitions behind 
the examples above, it does not matter. The point is that the sense of ‘the class of’ in which the above 
examples come out true seems to be a legitimate and widespread understanding of the expression in 
question and Mereology falls short of accounting for this plausibility.

6.4 Some more diﬃculties 
When expressions of the form α1 ε Kl(α2) are replaced by corresponding expressions 
of the form α1 ε α2 certainly some of the paradoxical assumptions are disproved. The 

187 
question is now: are we intuitively entitled to do so? Let us take a closer look at the 
justiﬁcation given by Soboci´nski. 
The expression ‘class(a)’ in the distributive sense is nothing more than a 
ﬁctitious name which replaces the well-known term of classical logic, ‘the 
extension of the objects a’. If one takes the term in this sense, the formula 
‘A ε Kl(a) ’ means the same thing as ‘A is an element of the extension of the 
objects a’ or, more brieﬂy, ‘A is a’. 
Soboci´nski does something quite unexpected here. For some reason he reads ‘ ε Kl(a)’ 
as ‘is an element of the extension of the objects a’. You certainly remember that Kl(a) 
is just the formal version of ‘ the extension of the name a ’. But if that is the case, ε 
is no longer being read by Soboci´nski as ‘is’, but rather as ‘is an element of’. However, 
this reading is plainly wrong given the interpretation given to ε by Le´sniewski. 
The above should suﬃce for rejecting the justiﬁcation that was given for the reduction. 
It does not constitute an argument against the reduction itself. So, here is an argument. 
Let us indeed concede that α ε Kl(β) is equivalent to α ε β. Then, (6.2) becomes: 
∀a,b[b ε el(a) ≡∃c(a ε c ∧ b ε c)] 
(6.31) 
which is certainly absurd. Take any two singular names, a, b. Clearly a ε a∪b∧b ε a∪b. 
Thus, ∃c(a ε c ∧ b ε c). But then, a ε el(b) and b ε el(a), that is, it turns out that any two 
individuals are elements of each other. This is a slightly inconvenient conclusion.12 
12Wait a second! Is not our eﬀort pointless? Is there really a need to introduce Kl? Is not Ontology a 
set theory already? Arguably, if one constructs a standard set-theoretic semantics for Ontology (name 
variables range over the power set of the domain of individuals etc.), something very much like the axiom 
of choice can be expressed in the language of Ontology by: 
∃f ∀a,b(a ε b → f(b) ε a) 
(6.32) 
which in the set-theoretic interpretation can be read as stating that there is an f such that for every 
non-empty set b, f(b) is an element of b (see (Davis 1975) for details). 
Davis himself uses set-theoretic paraphrases throughout his paper, commenting brieﬂy:

Set theoretic paraphrases will be used throughout this investigation since they are the most 
natural to use in discussions concerning the axiom of choice. (Davis 1975: 182) 
This line of reasoning is familiar. It resembles the standard argument to the eﬀect that the standard 
second-order logic is set theory (see section 7.3 for details). 
The fact that set-theoretic paraphrases are considered natural in a discussion does not imply that the 
paraphrased language indeed says something about sets. At most, one can be inclined to say that if it 
is assigned the standard set-theoretic semantics then it is possible to formulate in it formulas that are 
true in a model iﬀ a speciﬁc set-theoretic axiom (formulated in metalanguage) holds in the same model. 
This, in a sense, indicates that at least some facts usually stated in set theory may be taken to be 
expressible in the language of Ontology. The problem is that this does not answer the initial problem. 
Event hough formulas of Ontology may (in the standard set-theoretic semantics) have the same satis­
faction conditions as certain set-theoretic claims, the language of Ontology itself still does not contain a 
systematic device which would emulate the behavior of the usual set-theoretic jargon. I grant that there 
is a certain interesting translation along these lines: 
Class a is a subset of the class b 
∀c(c ε a → c ε b) 
Class a is empty 
¬∃bb ε a 
Classes a and b are disjoint 
¬∃c(c ε a ∧ c ε b) 
Still the language of Ontology is far from the generality enjoyed by the language of set theory. Let us 
take a closer look at (6.32). Take the common-sense set-theoretic semantics. We start with a possibly 
empty set of urelements, then construct the power set to obtain the range of name variables, and 
similarly for all other semantic categories. In this setting (6.32) says only that for any non-empty set 
of urelements there is a function that selects an element of that set. This is not the same as saying 
that for any nonempty family of pairwise disjoint nonempty sets there is a function which assigns to 
each element of that family a unique element of the input set. Of course, in Ontology we can emulate 
“nonempty family of pairwise disjoint nonempty sets of urelements” and similarly for any other “type” 
or “order” of the usual set-theoretic objects. But there is no single formula of Ontology which is true 
in a model if and only if the set-theoretic axiom of choice in its full generality is true in it. We have 
to introduce an axiom-of-choice-like formula for pretty much every semantic category separately. Now, 
since the language of Ontology has no upper limit on how complex semantic categories can get, this 
means that the best we could do is to suggest a choice schema under which all those particular choice 
axioms would fall. But this is quite diﬀerent from being able to express the axiom by means of a single 
formula of set theory.

6.6 Higher-order epsilon 
6.6.1 What is higher-order epsilon? 
Recall now that the question that launched the discussion in chapter 6 about distributive 
classes was: how to imitate the usual talk about distributive classes within Ontology? As 
we have already seen, Lesniewski–Soboci´nski’s solution to the Russell’s paradox did not 
work very well. An alternative approach has been suggested (Lejewski 1985). I introduce 
deﬁnitions of class-like operators and the predication between sets so that one can ﬁnd 
a sensible translation from the language of Set Theory to the language of Ontology that 
preserves provability. To mimic predication between sets a diﬀerent sort of functors is 
needed. A predication operators of this sort are called higher-order epsilon operators 
(HO ε ). I will start with explaining brieﬂy what these are. Then I will consider a few 
ways they can be introduced. Next, I will try to see what happens if one actually applies 
this strategy to Russell’s paradox. 

194 
Formulae (6.65)-(6.69) were given and the whole approach was suggested by Lejewski 
(1985) and advertised in a diﬀerent context by Henry. (Henry 1972: 42-44) Henry, after 
introducing the above formulae has argued that 
∃b(b ε b ≡¬b ε b) 
(6.64) 
is not a theorem. He was also quite optimistic about his conclusions: “This gives one 
acceptable sense to the principle that there are classes of whatever sorts of objects one 
may specify. (Here ‘are’ has its higher-order sense.)” (1972: 44) After giving (6.64) and 
denying it, he comments: “This is a contradiction which mays be viewed as a version of 
Russell’s paradox. It is of course avoided. . . In other words, intuitive clarity can be main­
tained throughout, this version of the paradox is avoided, and ad hoc evasive strategems 
of a formalist nature shown to be needless.” (1972: 46) The rest of this section pur­
sues some consequences and issues that (I think) arise from the consideration of these 
deﬁnitions, purporting to show that the issue is not as simple as Henry suggested. 
The basic idea is that instead of treating Kl(a) as a name of a class, we put it in a 
semantic category diﬀerent from the category of names and treat ‘is’ as ambiguous. 
For example, ‘Socrates is a philosopher’ is parsed as s ε P , but a sentence of a similar 
form, ‘Reading is learning’ is, if we follow Le´sniewskian intuitions, not a sentence directly 
about objects. Indeed, it is rather as if we said that anything of which the verb ‘read(s)’ 
may be truly predicated would be a subject of a true predication of the verb ‘learn(s)’. 
This sort of ambiguity can be systematized in Le´sniewski’s Ontology. Deﬁne the weak 
identity ﬁrst: 
∀a,b[ (a, b) ≡∀c(c ε a ≡ c ε b)] 
(6.65)
◦

195 
Now, every one-place sentential functor of a name argument can be nominalized:14 
∀a,φ[a ε trmhφi ≡ a ε a ∧ φ(a)] 
(6.66) 
There are at least two interesting deﬁnition of class. The ﬁrst introduces the class 
generated by a name: 
∀a,b[Cl[[a]](b) ≡◦(b, a)] 
(6.67) 
where ‘Cl[[A]](b)’ may be read as ‘b is the class of a’s’, and the other introduced the class 
generated by a condition: 
∀a,φ[Cl[[φ]](a) ≡ Cl[[trmhφi]](a)] 
(6.68) 
where ‘Cl[[φ]](a)’ may be read as ‘a is the class of those objects that φ-ize’. 
The corresponding higher-order epsilon ε1 that may allow us to talk about classes is 
deﬁned: 
∀φ,ψ[φ ε1 ψ ≡∃a(φ(a) ∧ ψ(a)) ∧∀b,c(φ(b) ∧ φ(c) →◦(b, c))] 
(6.69) 
which, if you deﬁne: 
∀φ[exthφi ≡∀b,c(φ(b) ∧ φ(c) →◦(b, c))] 
(6.70) 
where exthφi may be read as ‘φ’ determines a unique possible name, up to coextensiveness 
(or, in other words, φ determines a class) may be expressed by: 
∀φ,ψ[φ ε1 ψ ≡∃a(φ(a) ∧ ψ(a)) ∧ exthφi)] 
(6.71) 
The basic idea is that φ ε1 ψ is true if there is only one (up to coextensiveness) name 
of which φ can be truly predicated and it is one of the names of which ψ can be truly 
predicated.

semantic categories, I will just arbitrarily pick diﬀerent shapes for diﬀerent categories for the purposes 
of this chapter.

6.6.2 HO ε and Russell’s paradox (outlined) 
Let us temporarily assume that we can mimic the talk about distributive classes using the 
above strategy. What happens if we employ it to discuss Russell’s paradox as formulated 
by Le´sniewski and Soboci´nski? Here is a sketch of the discussion which will follow. 
Take the paradoxical axioms from section 6.1. Can they be interpreted using the 
above strategy? It turns out that we have to play around with the language in order to 
be able to translate them into the language of Ontology. Also, there is no unique possible 
way of massaging the axioms into the language of Ontology. (i) One of the options entails 
that only if a is a singular term there exists the class of a’s, which means that this read­
ing falls short of imitating the usual set-theoretic talk. (ii) Another way of translating 
the axioms turns (6.4) into a formula which entails that a ε Cl[[b]] is equivalent to a ε b, 
which is not a plausible identiﬁcation if we want to stick with the distributive reading 
(without running into the already discussed conclusion that for any two individuals one 
is an element of another). Thus, it seems, in this reading we lose the extensionality of 
distributive sets (which generally is not a good news if we want to imitate the usual 
language of set theory). (iii) Finally, I discuss yet another quite natural reading. Inter­
estingly, translating formulas that were contradictory on the previous reading using this 
strategy yields a set of theorems of Ontology (which we have good reasons to believe to 
be consistent). 
Here, I end the inquiry, and the moral from the story has a few components. First, 
some strategies of using higher-order epsilon to imitate the usual talk of set theory have 
to be rejected. Second, there is a quite natural higher-epsilon rendering of initially 
contradictory axioms where we obtain a consistent set of theorems of Ontology. The 
latter fact can be interpreted in at least two distinct ways: we can either say that this 
shows that the translation is inadequate because it does not preserve the provability 
operation (in which case we are left with nothing besides a criticism of higher-epsilon 

198 
strategies), or we can say that there is something intuitive about this translation and treat 
the question how the provability relation changes with the translation as interesting in 
itself. Now that a philosophical guide has been sketched, let us get back to the trenches. 
6.6.3 HO ε and Russell’s paradox (technically speaking)

15What makes a connective a higher-order epsilon is an interesting question. The intuition is that f

This however is far from providing a formally correct and precise deﬁnition.

6.7 S lupecki’s generalized mereology 
6.7.1 System M 
S lupecki (1958)18 attempted to extend Le´sniewski’s mereology in order to obtain a mere­
ological equivalent of set theory. As he put it: 
Generalizing mereology, I keep in mind the following two aims: 
(a) in the generalized system the notion of a set should preserve its intuitive 
sense; speciﬁcally sets should be deﬁned in a way that treats them as concrete 
objects. 
(b) the generalized system of mereology should be able to underlie mathemat­
ics to the same extent to which simple type theory or axiomatic set theory 
does. This purpose has not been attained by Le´sniewski’s own system, in 
which, for instance, natural numbers arithmetic cannot be built. (S lupecki 
1958: 156)19 
S lupecki, instead of Ontology (with name variables a, b, c, . . . uses a language with 
individual variables x, y, z, . . . predicate variables f, g, f1, g1, . . . and general functor vari­
ables φ, φ1, . . . (whose category is to be contextually salient), and he often uses formulas 
with free variables as if the free variables occurring in them were bound by a universal 
quantiﬁer standing in the front of the formula. All his axioms mimic Le´sniewski’s axioms 
for Mereology. He assumes the reﬂexivity and transitivity of (improper) parthood rela­

(6.132)

It follows that system M, and even more the whole system of generalized 
mereology suﬃces as a system of foundations of mathematics, because – as 
it is well-known – S1 suﬃces for that purpose. This conclusion may seem 
trivial because the full system of simple type theory without any additions 
can constitute a foundation of mathematics. However, in my paper I give 
arguments (I realize that those arguments are not wholly convincing and for 
that reason I do not repeat them here) indicating that constants belonging 
to semantics categories to which variables f, φ, Φ, . . . belong are not names 
of sets or any other objects, but rather expressions devoid of independent 
reference. Those terms cannot denote concrete objects, so the full system 
of simple type theory does not fulﬁll aim (a), which was mentioned above.

6.7.2 Diﬃculties 
Notice ﬁrst that introducing (6.131) is a technical trick which is not intuitively justiﬁed. 
Not only does the theory cease to be, in a sense, ontologically neutral (that is, it did 
not require that there exist any object before), but also, philosophically speaking, there

seems to be no object which we could pick out and determine to do the job of the empty 
set. Clearly, on Le´sniewski’s view, technical niceties that this move provides do not 
constitute a reason for departing from our intuitions about empty set. And one of the 
main intuitions is that if we think of sets mereologically there is no reason to say that 
there is an empty set (and if we do not think of sets mereologically, it seems clear that 
the empty set is not just an arbitrarily picked and ﬁxed concrete object). 
Another counterintuitive fact is this. The S lupecki class determined by a “property” 
φ is just the fusion of all the objects that have it (if anything has that property at all). 
For instance, deﬁne a name constant U: 
∀a(a ε U ≡ a ε a ∧ (a ε a ∨¬a ε a)) 
(6.146) 
The object ClS (U), with respect to a ﬁxed domain of discourse is just the fusion of all

(6.148)

∀a[a ε Δφ0 ≡ (a ε a ∧∃b(φ0(b) ∧ a ε ClS (b)))] 
That is, 
∀a[a ε Δφ0 ≡ (a ε a ∧∃b((b ε b ∧ (b ε b ∨¬b ε b) ∧ a ε ClS (b))))] 
∀a[a ε Δφ0 ≡ (a ε a ∧∃b(b ε b ∧ a ε ClS (b)))] 
But if a ε a then also a ε ClS (a). Hence: 
∀a[a ε Δφ0 ≡ a ε a] 
(6.149) 
Also, 
∀a[a ε Δφ00 ≡ (a ε a ∧∃b(φ00(b) ∧ a ε ClS (b)))]]

But since if a ε a then a ε ClS (a), it also follows that if a ε a then ∃b(a ε ClS (b)). Therefore: 
∀a[a ε Δφ00 ≡ a ε a] 
(6.150) 
(6.144) together with (6.147) imply that: 
∀a(ClS (a) ∈2 ClS (Δφ0 ) ≡ φ0(a) ≡ a ε a)

∀a(Cls(a) ∈2 ClS (Δφ00 ≡ φ00(a) ≡ a ε a ∨¬a ε a) 
But this indicates that at least certain instances of the indiscernibility of identicals have 
to be rejected. In this case, we should reject: 
∀a,φ,ψ[ClS (Δφ) = ClS (Δψ)
(ClS (a) ∈2 ClS (Δφ) ≡ ClS (a) ∈2 ClS (Δψ))] 
(6.151)
→ 
Otherwise, the theory would imply that ∀aa ε a, which contradicts an already provable 
theorem of Ontology: ∃a¬a ε a. 
Therefore it seems S lupecki’s generalized mereology has certain ﬂaws that precludes 
it from being a good candidate for a nominalist replacement for the language of any 
mainstream set theory. Even if the theory was devoid of oddities of the kind mentioned 
above, it still would not be obvious that it follows the Le´sniewskian agenda and satisﬁes 
S lupecki’s requirements. For the worry brought up by S lupecki remains: simple type 
theory without mereological extensions can serve as foundation of mathematics (in a 
sense) and unless a nominalistically satisfying account of higher-order quantiﬁers and 
variables is given, the question whether the system works as advertised is open. 

Chapter 7 
Modal interpretation of plural quantiﬁers 
7.1 Introduction 
Le´sniewski was a nominalist and he constructed Protothetic, Ontology and Mereology in 
his endeavor to provide a nominalistically acceptable foundations of mathematics. Yet, 
a philosophical question arises, even simpler than the question whether the systems are 
suﬃciently rich to capture certain intuitions that we have about sethood, the question 
whether the logical part of the theory (Protothetic and Ontology) is ontologically innocent 
and does not commit one to the existence of abstract objects. 
The question is: can a nominalist use Le´sniewski’s quantiﬁcation, especially in Ontol­
ogy? What, for instance, do name variables in Ontology range over? Le´sniewski himself 
did not provide any intuitive explanation of his quantiﬁers and the ﬁrst stab might be 
that it is subsets of the domain of individuals that have to be values of bound variables 
of this sort. If this really is the case, anyone who uses the system will fail in providing a 
nominalistic reconstruction of mathematics. 
In this sense, the question of how Le´sniewski’s quantiﬁers can be interpreted without 
commitment to abstract objects is prior to the question whether (a decent part of) 
mathematics can be reconstructed within his systems. In this chapter I focus on the 
question of ontological commitment of Le´sniewski’s quantiﬁcation. First, I introduce the 
language of Quantiﬁed Name Logic (QNL) which, practically is just a very basic part 
of Le´sniewski’s Ontology1 (section 7.2.1) and provide it with a set–theoretic semantics 
(section 7.2.2). Next, I compare QNL with Boolos’s logic of plurals and prove that the 
1Basically, the one that we get when we admit name variables and quantiﬁers binding those variables 
only. 
214 

215 
expressive power of both languages (modulo set–theoretic semantics) is the same (section 
7.2.3). 
Having done that, I discuss the question whether plural quantiﬁcation commits its 
users to sets. I start with a “phony” argument to the eﬀect that it does (section 7.3). 
It ultimately rests on the assumption that the logic obtained with the substitutional se­
mantics is devoid of certain theoretically preferable features that QNL with set–theoretic 
semantics has. 
This is the assumption that I challenge in section 7.4. In order to do that I develop a 
substitutional semantics which avoids the objections raised in section 7.3. First, I discuss 
two earlier attempts of providing plural quantiﬁcation with ontologically innocent seman­
tics. In section 7.4.1 I present combinatorial semantics (introduced by Peter Simons) and 
indicate its weak points. In section 7.4.2 Rickey’s natural models are deﬁned. Unfortu­
nately it is quite probable that Rickey’s semantics does not avoid the objections against 
substitutional reading of quantiﬁers raised before. 
In section 7.4.3 I develop a Kripke semantics for the logic of plurals (some of basic 
ideas stem from the considerations of Simons and Rickey). It is a modal interpretation, 
where the plural quantiﬁer ‘∃aφ’ (suppose φ does not contain free variables other than 
a) is intuitively read as ‘it is possible to introduce a name a which would make φ substi­
tutionally true.’ There are good reasons to claim that QNL with Kripke semantics has 
the same expressive power as QNL with set–theoretic semantics (as it turns out, for any 
set–theoretic model there is a Kripke model which agrees with it on all QNL formulas 
and the other way round: for any Kripke model there is a set–theoretic model which 
agrees with it on all formulas). 
Next, in section 7.4.4 I explore the modal factor involved in this semantics by develop­
ing a two–sorted ﬁrst–order modal logic of naming (MLN). I show that QNL with Kripke 
semantics can be embedded in the language of MLN. Since this embedding preserves 

216 
models (i.e. models for QNL and MLN are the same, what changes is just the inter­
pretation of symbols), it seems that the ontological commitment of QNL with Kripke 
semantics does not go beyond the ontological commitment of ﬁrst–order (two–sorted) 
modal logic (with one relation constant). Since it is much less plausible that ﬁrst–order 
modal logic commits one to abstract objects than that plural quantiﬁcation does, this 
strengthen my case for the ontological innocence of QNL. 
The last section of this chapter (7.4.5) contains philosophical comments regarding 
the content of previous sections. I brieﬂy compare my approach to Chihara’s theory of 
constructibility quantiﬁers, discuss the issues pertaining to the ontological commitment, 
show how this approach avoids the usual objections against substitutional interpretation 
of plural quantiﬁers, and defend the view that there is nothing philosophically suspicious 
about restricting admissible models to complete naming structures (a notion to be deﬁned 
in section 7.4.3). 
7.2 Quantiﬁed name logic 
7.2.1 The language 
Variables ranging over tuples of symbols (or of tuples of tuples, etc.) from a contextually 
salient alphabet are σ’s with (or, in one case, without) numerical subscripts.2 
A formal language L can be thought of as a tuple hA, Σi, where A is a countable 
alphabet of L (containing brackets) and Σ is its basic signature. The elements of A 
which are not brackets will be called ‘letters’. 
The set of types T is deﬁned by the following conditions: (i) hsi, hni ∈ T , (ii) if 
σ, σ1, . . . , σn ∈ T , then hσ, σ1, . . . , σni ∈ T . Often instead of hsi, hti we will just write s, t. 
A basic signature Σ of a language L is a function from L’s alphabet without the 
2Basically: those tuples that can be “built” from elements of this alphabet given that tuples can 
occur in tuples. 

217 
brackets (i.e. the set of all non-bracket expressions of L which are not further meaning­
fully decomposable into simpler expressions of L) into T . A signature relative to a set of 
symbols A, ΣA is a function mapping L’s alphabet into a set of types that can be built 
from elements of A. 
The signature function assigns to every non-bracket symbol of L its syntactic type. 
Intuitively, s is the type of sentences and n is the type of names, which are the two basic 
types (for now). A tuple hσ, σ1, . . . , σni is the type of those expressions which applied to 
n arguments of types (respectively) σ1, . . . , σn yield a complex expression of type σ. 
We construct the set of well-formed expressions of L and extend the signature func­
tion. Letters of L are well-formed expressions of L. If τ, τ1, . . . , τn are well-formed 
expressions of L, Σ(τi) = σi (1 ≤ i ≤ n), and Σ(τ) = hσ, σ1, . . . , σni then τ(τ1, . . . , τn) is 
a well-formed expression of L of type σ. 3 If a well-formed expression of L is of type s, it 
is a well-formed formula of L. 
The language that we will be working with in this chapter is generated by the alphabet 
containing brackets, name variables a1, a2, a3, . . . (as an abbreviation, also a, b, c, d, 
possibly with numerical subscripts), all of type n, the constant ε of type hs, n, ni, the 
existential quantiﬁer ∃ of type hs, n, si (the universal quantiﬁer ∀ has its usual deﬁnition). 
The restriction on the use of quantiﬁers is quite obvious: the argument of type n cannot 
be a constant. Here this will not bother us unless we introduce constants into our 
language. Also, I will write bound variables in the subscript of the quantiﬁer and write 
multiple bound variables after a single quantiﬁer instead of writing multiple quantiﬁers. 
The negation symbol 
is of type hs, si and the conjunction symbol ∧ of type hs, s, si.
¬ 
Other propositional connectives are deﬁned as usual. 
If we denote the language under present discussion by ‘QNL ε ’, the above yields the 
usual inductive deﬁnition. The set of well-formed formulas of QNL ε is the least set 
3With a slight proviso for the quantiﬁer, read on for details. 

218 
satisfying the following conditions: 
1. If α1, α2 are name variables, ε (α1, α2) is a well-formed formula. 
2. If φ1, φ2 are well-formed formulas and α is a name variable, also 
(φ1), ∧(φ1, φ2)
¬
and ∀αφ1 are well-formed formulas. 
As variables standing for well-formed formulas (the context will determine what lan­
guage we are talking about) I will use φ, ψ, possibly with numerical subscripts. I will also 
use inﬁx notation for the epsilon and connectives and drop brackets according to usual 
conventions (in terms of the binding strength, 
is ﬁrst and ∧, ∨, →, ≡ follow).

7.2.2 Set-theoretic semantics 
Let us start with quite an imparsimonious but fairly standard semantics for QNL ε . Take 
the domain to be a set of objects and take the range of name variables to be the powerset 
of the domain. An S-model of QNL ε is a pair hD, Ii such that D is an arbitrary set 
and I is a total function which maps name variables into the powerset of D (i.e. to each 
name variable it assigns a subset of the domain). 
Neither D nor I(a) for any a has to be non-empty. Let α, β be meta-variables ranging 
over name variables and let φ, ψ be meta-variables ranging over wﬀs. 
We can deﬁne the truth in an S- model as follows:

7.2.3 QNL ε and Boolos’ logic of plurals 
The logic of plurals has been ﬁrst developed by Boolos (1998a), but in presenting its 
syntax I will follow a slightly more regimented formulation given by Linnebo (2004).4 
The pure5 language of the logic of plurals (LP) has among its terms singular 
variables (x, x1, x2, . . .) and plural variables (xx, xx1, xx2, . . .). It contains two 2-place 
logical predicates: the standard individual identity symbol = and the is one of operator 
. If xi, xj are individual variables, x = y is a well–formed formula. If x is an individual 
variable and xx a plural variable, x  xx is a well–formed formula. 
φ and φ ∧ ψ, ∃xφ
¬
and ∃xxφ are well-formed formulas if φ and ψ are, x is an individual variable and xx is a 
plural variable. Nothing else is a well–formed formula. The deﬁnitions of other Boolean 
connectives are standard. 
Boolos proposed his system as an alternative to second order languages which on 
their face are committed to the existence of sets, and so did not give a set theoretic 
semantics. Such a semantics, however, is easily given.6 An LP–model is a structure 
hD, R, P i where D is a non-empty domain of objects, R is the reference function that 
maps individual variables into D and P is the plural interpretation function which maps 
plural variables into the set of nonempty subsets of D. 7 
4Linnebo, not without a reason, dubbed it a “plural ﬁrst-order” language, but I will avoid calling

predicates as a group separate from plural terms.

requirement slightly and demand only that there be at least one object satisfying φ(x). (Most people 
who write on the subject make this concession.)” (Linnebo 2004: sect. 1.2). Burgess (2004: 197),

The following can be proven by induction:

t(xi = xj ) = ai ε aj ∧ aj ε ai 
Next, we claim: if hD, R, P i |= xi = xj then hD, Ii |= t(xi = xj ). For indeed assume the

R(xi) ∈ P (xxj ) and therefore hD, R, P i |= xi  xxj . 
The case of Boolean connectives is fairly obvious. Let’s turn to quantiﬁed formulas, 
then. Assume that for any LP-model M the formula φ holds at it if and only if t(φ) 
holds at the M 0 which is t-generated from M. Suppose hD, R, P i |= ∃xi φ. Then there 
is an R0 such that R0 diﬀers from R at most at xi such that hD, R0, P i |= φ. Hence,

hD, I0i |= t(φ) (where hD, I0i is t-generated from hD, R0, P i). Also, since hD, I0i is a 
t-generated model, |I0(ai)|= 1. Hence hD, I0i |= ai ε ai. Take hD, Ii t-generated from 
hD, R, P i. I0 diﬀers from I at most in what it assigns to ai. So: hD, Ii |= ∃ai (ai ε ai∧t(φ)), 
i.e. hD, Ii |= t(∃xi φ). 
On the other hand, assume hD, Ii |= t(∃xi φ). That is, hD, Ii |= ∃ai (ai ε ai ∧ t(φ)). 
So for some ai-variant of I, I0 : hD, I0i |= ai ε ai ∧ t(φ). Since hD, Ii was a t-generated

LP-model from which it is generated: hD, R0, P i. Clearly hD, R0, P i |= φ. If hD, R, P i is 
the LP-model from which hD, Ii is t-generated, then R0 diﬀers from R at most in what 
it assigns to xi, and hence hD, R, P i |= ∃xi (φ). 
The argument for the plural quantiﬁer is similar. Assume hD, R, P i |= ∃xxi φ. Then 
there is a model hD, R, P 0i which models φ such that P 0 is an xxi-variant of P . Take 
a model which is t-generated from hD, R, P 0i, hD, I0i. By the induction hypothesis 
hD, I0i |= t(φ). Since I0 is t-generated from R and P 0, it has to assign a non-empty 
subset of D to bi. Hence hD, I0i |= ∃aa ε bi. 8 So hD, I0i |= ∃aa ε bi ∧ t(φ). The model 
t-generated from hD, R, P i, hD, Ii diﬀers from hD, I0i in what it assigns to bi. So 
hD, Ii |= ∃bi (∃aa ε bi ∧ t(φ)), i.e., hD, I |= t(∃xxi φ). 
For the opposite direction, assume hD, Ii is t-generated from hD, R, P i and 
hD, Ii |= t(∃xxi φ), i.e. hD, Ii |= ∃bi (∃aa ε bi ∧ t(φ)). So for some I0 which is a bi-variant of 
I, hD, I0i |= ∃aa ε bi ∧t(φ). But this means that hD, I0i, even if it diﬀers from hD, Ii is still 
a t-generated model (it assigns a non-empty subset of D to the only variable on which 
it possibly diﬀers from hD, Ii). Take the LP-model from which hD, I0i is t-generated, 
hD, R, P 0i. Clearly hD, R, P 0i |= φ (by the induction hypothesis). P 0 diﬀers from P at 
most in what it assigns to xxi. Hence hD, R, P i |= ∃xxi φ.

Notice that in the translation of a formula, the ﬁrst conjunct in the antecedent re­
stricts possible counterexamples to non-empty S–models, all other conjuncts ensure that 
translations of free individual variables of φ are taken to “name” unique objects and 
translations of free plural variables “refer” to a non-empty plurality. For this reason: 
Theorem 10. For any LP–formula φ, φ is LP–valid if and only if trans(φ) is S–valid. 
Proof. This follows from theorem 9. Suppose an LP–formula is not valid. So it fails at 
a certain LP-model, M. But then trans(φ) fails at M’s t-generated model M 0. On the 
other hand suppose trans(φ) is not S-valid. By deﬁnition of validity, it obviously follows 
that it fails at a certain model, N 0. Unfortunately it does not follow that N 0 actually 
is a t–generated model of any LP –model. Hence, we have to show how from N 0 we can 
construct a t–generated model which agrees with N 0 on trans(φ). 
Since N 0 satisﬁes the antecedent of trans(φ) (to falsify a conditional it has to make 
its antecedent true), there is a t-generated model N 00 where trans(φ) fails. Basically 
N 00 assigns to all the relevant variables (variables free in the antecedent of trans(φ)) the 
same subsets of the domain which N 0 assigned to them and then assigns singletons to 
all irrelevant aj ’s and non-empty subsets of the domain to all irrelevant bj ’s. Clearly 
N 00 |= t(φ). Take the LP-model from which N 00 is t-generated, N. By theorem 9, 
N = φ. 
There is an interesting relation that goes the other way round, via monadic second 
order logic (MSOL). The language of MSOL contains a denumerable list of individual 
variables x1, x2, x3, . . ., a denumerable list of predicate variables P1, P2, . . ., Boolean con­
nectives, quantiﬁers binding individual and predicate variables and the identity predicate 
=. An MSOL model is a tuple hD, r, pi where D is a non-empty domain of objects, r is 
a function that maps individual variables to elements of D and p is a function that maps 
predicate letters to subsets of D.

Theorem 11. For any QNL ε formula φ, ∃aa ε a → φ is valid iﬀ tr(φ) is MSOL-valid.

This is relevant to the relation between QNL ε and LP because if one takes standard 
(Boolos’) proof–theoretic formulation of LP and a standard proof theoretic formulation 
of MSOL, there is a translation from MSOL to LP which preserves theoremhood (in both 
directions). See (Linnebo 2004: sect. 1.3) for details. 
It is worth remarking that providing QNL ε or LP with a set–theoretic semantics 
(arguably) deprives them of the expressive power that it has been argued the natural 
language plural quantiﬁcation has. A classical argument is this. The following is intu­
itively true: 
(7.32) 
There are some objects such that all and only those objects that are 
sets and not elements of themselves are among them. 
The following, however, cannot be the case (the sentence states the existence of Russell’s 
set): 
(7.33) There is a set of objects such that all and only those objects that are 
sets and not elements of themselves are elements of it. 
(7.32) seems to state the existence of a certain plurality (the plurality of those objects 
that are sets and not elements of themselves). So, prima facie, there are pluralities 
which are not sets. Hence, quantiﬁcation over pluralities cannot be fully captured by 
interpretation which reads it as quantiﬁcation over sets (or subsets of a domain). 
For now, without any attempt to explain the use of plurals which does not seem to be 
captured by the set–theoretic semantics I will just remark that the expressivity provided 
by the set–theoretic semantics is complex enough to raise problems that will be my main 
concern. 

227 
7.3 Plural quantiﬁcation and ontological commitment 
Before we turn to the question as to whether we are ontologically committed to sets 
when we use QNL ε , a few words of explanation regarding the notion of ontological 
commitment are due. I take (interpreted) theories to be the primary bearers of ontological 
commitment. People become ontologically committed when they accept ontologically 
committed theories. I do not accept Quine’s criterion of ontological commitment: “take 
a ﬁrst-order formulation of a theory — the theory is committed to whatever has to be in 
the range of quantiﬁcation in the resulting ﬁrst-order theory.”9 Unfortunately I do not 
think that there is a “criterion” of ontological commitment that both (i) analyzes our 
intuitions about ontological commitment correctly and (ii) is epistemically more tractable 
than the notion of ontological commitment itself.10 Speciﬁcally, I will discuss the question 
of whether a logic is committed (or commits one) to sets. What is the meaning of the 
phrase ‘a logic commits one to sets’? This means that even if someone’s prior overall 
beliefs did not commit one to sets, one becomes committed to sets when one accepts a 
given logic as a reasonable tool for one’s practical and theoretical purposes. For now, let 
me adopt the following rough–and–ready requirement: a logic is committed to sets if (not 
necessarily iﬀ) its semantics11 requires that certain expression of its language refer to sets 
if its theorems are to be valid. If an equally successful explanation can be given which 
does not require the existence of sets I will say that the logic (with this new semantics) 
does not commit one to sets. 
What I will call the received view is that any higher–order logic bears commitment 
to abstract objects (usually sets). One argument for this conclusion is that higher–order 
logics prove theorems whose content is set–theoretic (or which are valid if and only if 
certain theorems of set theory are true), and since set theory is committed to abstract 
9I mostly agree with the criticism of this sort of approach to be found in (Chihara 1973, 1990)). 
10Although the issue lies beyond the scope of my present considerations. 
11And I take a logic to be given together with a semantics, more remarks on this issue will follow. 

228 
objects (sets), so is the logic. I will get back to arguments of this sort. Before I do that, 
however, I will consider another possible way of defending the same view. One could 
imagine someone arguing as follows. 
Whenever we present a logical system we have to provide it with a formal 
semantics for it that we ﬁnd adequate. When we use a logical system with 
a certain semantics we are committed to whatever the quantiﬁers of the lan­
guage of the system under considerations range over according to the se­
mantics we provided. The logic of plurals (or QNL ε, for that matter) can 
have two kinds of semantics: a set–theoretic semantics (discussed before) or 
a substitutional semantics. QNL ε with substitutional semantics is not theo­
retically satisfactory. Therefore it has to be given a set–theoretic semantics. 
But on the set–theoretic reading of plural quantiﬁers, they range over subsets 
of a domain. Subsets of a domain are abstract objects and hence whenever 
we use the logic of plurals we are committed to abstract objects. 
Let us dub arguments that go along these lines semantical arguments for the claim 
that higher order quantiﬁcation commits one to abstract objects. 
I have not met an argument formulated exactly this way, but it seems that the ten­
dency can be traced back at least to Quine. In (1947) he remarks that any usage of general 
terms in the context of quantiﬁcation commits one to abstract objects (pp. 74–75), and 
he explicitly says about quantiﬁers binding predicate letters: 
If we bind the schematic predicate–letters of quantiﬁcation theory, we achieve 
a reiﬁcation of universals which no device analogous to Fitch’s is adequate 
to explaining away. These universals are entities whereof predicates may 
thenceforward be regarded as names; they may be construed as attributes or 
as classes . . . The predicate letters, when thus admitted to quantiﬁers, acquire 

229 
the status of variables taking classes as values. (pp. 77-78) 
He is even more explicit when he discusses Le´sniewski’s Ontology (Quine 1952: 141). 
There, he criticizes Ajdukiewicz and Le´sniewski for not attaching suﬃcient signiﬁcance 
to the fact that “the variables which have been said to stand in places appropriate 
to general terms are subjected in Le´sniewski’s theory to quantiﬁcation.” This theory, 
according to Quine, “. . . surely commits Le´sniewski to a realm of values of his variables 
of quantiﬁcation; and all his would–be general terms must be viewed as naming these 
values singly.” Quite rhetorically Quine claims that if this quantiﬁcation is not to be 
read as committing Le´sniewski to classes, then he is “at a loss to imagine wherein such 
commitment even on the part of a professing Platonist can consist”.12 
Quine’s argument is, however, slightly diﬀerent from the one I gave. He is already 
assuming that being subjected to quantiﬁcation automatically carries ontological commit­
ment, and instead of explicitly discussing the need for semantics and how what semantics 
we choose can impact our ontological commitment he insists that sentences should be 
put in their standard ﬁrst–order form.13 
A train of thought which more explicitly relies on the relevance of the choice of a 
12On Le´sniewski’s view, ontological commitments are made by using the ε operator, we can guess 
what the answer to Quine’s question would Le´sniewski give. For Le´sniewski, one would be committed 
to abstract objects if, for instance, she accepted a theory which entails ‘for some a, a is (where ‘is’ has 
the same meaning as ε ) a set (where ‘set’ is given a meaning that at least entails that sets are abstract 
object), or if she uttered something like ‘at least one object is a set’ (where the quantiﬁcation is not 
ranging over sets, and the form of the statement is ‘∃aa ε Set’). 
13Quine (1970: 66-67) gave yet another argument. In ordinary ﬁrst–order quantiﬁcations (like the one 
expressed by ‘(∃x)(x walks)’) the open sentence displays a variable in a position where a name could 
stand. The quantiﬁed sentences are not read as quantifying over names: they rather quantify over objects 
that could be named by a name placed in the position of a variable. Similarly when we bind predicate 
variable, we “treat predicate positions suddenly as name positions, and hence . . . treat predicates as 
names of entities of some sort.” This argument has been dealt with by Boolos (1998b: 37-39), who 
points out that to put a predicate variable in a quantiﬁer may require that the variable be treated as 
having a range but we do not have to treat predicate positions as name positions just because ordinary 
individual variables do so. Boolos’s discussion in 1998b, however, is not concerned with what I want to 
discuss. He was arguing against Quine’s claim that second–order logic is set theory in sheep’s clothing, 
which is a stronger claim than that second–order logic is committed to sets. Even Boolos admits that 
“There is a clear sense . . . in which second–order logic can at least be said to be committed to the 
assertion that an empty set exists.” (Boolos 1998b: 47).

semantics can be found in (Burgess 2004: 217): 
. . . if a model theory has not yet been developed of a given logical notion, it 
may be alleged that the notion is ‘meaningless’ because it lacks a ‘semantics’. 
On the other hand, once a model theory has been developed for a given 
logical notion it may be alleged that problematic ‘ontological commitments’ 
are implicit in the use of the notion. . . 
Let us return to the semantical argument. The reasoning can be blocked by rejecting 
one of the premises. Which one can we reasonably reject, if we have strong nominalist 
inclinations? Consider the ﬁrst premise: 
(7.34) 
For any logical system a formal semantics is needed. 
We could try rejecting this one. One way of reading Boolos is that he thinks plural 
quantiﬁcation is primitive and it is intuitively clear that it does not commit its user to 
classes.14 He does develop a certain semantics where plural variables are not interpreted 
as taking classes as values, but this is a wholly diﬀerent issue. When he does that, he 
is already assuming the existence of sets and attempts to use plural quantiﬁcation as an 
alternative to taking second–order set-theoretic variables to range over (possibly) proper 
classes.15 
14He says: “ “There are some critics etc.” doesn’t, it seems, commit us, in any ordinary sense of the 
word “commit,” to the existence of a class of critics; what it commits us to, one would have thought, 
is, as we have noted, some critics none of whom etc. We are forced by Quine’s criterion to say that it 
commits us to a class; but why, we should ask, should we accept the criterion? If it is answered: because 
it’s Quine’s phrase, and he is at liberty to deﬁne it as he pleases, then we should rejoin: if so, Quine 
is deﬁning a relation which holds between us and certain objects (in this case, classes) whose existence 
the normal use of our word does not force us to admit, and hence a relation that ought not to be called 
“ontological commitment” . . . The statements do not appear to commit us to classes; why believe that 
it is their translation into the notation of ﬁrst–order logic augmented with variables ranging over classes 
that determines what they are actually committed to?” (Boolos 1998a: 77-78) 
15This does not mean that ordinarily second-order variables range over proper classes. It is rather 
the peculiarity of second-order set theory that Boolos is considering as an alternative to his set theory 
that its ﬁrst-order variables range over sets and second–order variables range over (possibly proper) 
classes. “In the case of a second–order language, such as the second–order language of set theory, there 
are at least two diﬀerent sorts of truth–theory that can be given: on one of these, it would be quite 
natural to deﬁne “value” so that the second–order variables would turn out to have classes as values [. . . ] 

231 
Similarly, Le´sniewski might be interpreted as treating his quantiﬁcation as primitive. 
He did not develop any semantics for his systems and there is no textual evidence as to 
him providing a clear account of his quantiﬁers. Burgess (2004: 217) seems to be taking 
the same primitivist position: 
. . . I maintain that even if no one ever did present a satisfactory model theory 
for plural logic, the plural was in systematic use in natural languages long 
before model theory for anything had been born or thought, and such long– 
standing systematic usages are meaningful if anything is. 
Quite likely Burgess is right when he says that logical devices can be meaningful even 
if they have not been given a semantical account. In the end, people constructed and 
used logical systems long before the notion of a formal semantics had been developed. It 
does not, however, mean that it is good practice nowadays to construct logical systems 
without providing them with a formal account of the meaning of the devices used in those 
systems. Formal semantics of various systems perform important theoretical tasks. Even 
if having a formal semantics does not have to make a diﬀerence between the meaningless 
and the meaningful, a semantics certainly clariﬁes the meaning of logical devices (like 
quantiﬁers). Having a formal semantics of a system allows us to get a better handle 
on what the system works like and on all the interesting properties that a system may 
have with respect to this semantics. One can hardly deny that the completeness of ﬁrst– 
order logic, the completeness of second–order logic with respect to Henkin semantics 
or the incompleteness of standard second–order logic are important properties of those 
logics and that these results provide us with a certain insight into the nature of those 
systems.16 Even more importantly, some sort of explanation is needed when the pluralist 
The present theory, however, makes no explicit mention of sequences whose values are (proper classes). 
It does not proceed by introducing functions that assign to each second-order variable a unique class, 
possibly proper.” (Boolos 1998a: 78–81). 
16Simons (1985b: 196-197) raises a similar question: does Ontology need a formal semantics? His 
answer is positive, but his argument is diﬀerent. He says that even if the expressions of Ontology are 

232 
(i.e. a proponent of the logic of plurals) who claims that she is not committed to abstract 
objects is challenged by the Platonist. If instead of providing a clear account of how plural 
quantiﬁcation is to be understood and why it does not commit one to abstract objects 
she says that it is primitive and people have been using it for ages, she practically refuses 
to join the discussion and they reach an impasse. Hence, it seems that we have good 
reasons to accept (7.34). 
Consider now the second premise: 
(7.35) 
If we use a certain logical system and believe that a certain semantics 
is an adequate semantics of this language, we are committed to whatever lies 
in the range(s) of quantiﬁcation according to this semantics. 
This, on the face of it, is quite uncontroversial. When we give a semantics that explains 
truth–conditions of a sentence in terms of (some sort of) semantic role of its constituents 
and we believe that the semantics explains them adequately, we must be convinced that 
we got the role of the constituents right. Hence, prima facie, if we provide our logic 
with a simple substitutional semantics with a determinate class of substituents we are 
committed to their existence. If, on the other hand, we give a set–theoretic semantics 
for second–order logic, it seems, we are committed to sets. It does not mean, of course, 
that whenever one uses set–theoretic semantics for second–order logic one is committed 
to sets. It is only when one claims that this is an adequate semantics of that language 
(one that explains correctly the semantic role of expressions of the language) that the 
semantics has to be taken seriously.17 
meaningful, “a theory of a language . . . is incomplete if it does not include . . . an account of what their 
being meaningful consists in”, and that is why a theory of Ontology should be enriched with a semantics. 
To my mind, this is more of a restatement of the claim that is being argued for than a real argument. 
17The notion of ‘adequacy’ as used here is somewhat vague, so a few words of explanation. Its use 
mirrors the distinction between what happens when one employs a certain mathematical theory to pro­
vide a formal semantics for a system but nevertheless treats it more like a mathematically interesting or 
inspiring exercise rather than an explanation of what certain constituents of the subject language mean, 
and what happens when one uses certain mathematical theory to explain the meaning of those devices. 
The point is, a nominalist could still play the game of giving set–theoretic semantics for second–order 

233 
There is a small catch here. The substitutional semantics commits one to names 
only if the way it is formulated requires that there be (in a model) a determined set of 
substituents. On the other hand, a substitutional semantics where variables range over 
certain possible expressions does not obviously commit one to their existence, just like 
saying that there is a possibility of error does not commit one to the existence of possible 
errors. I will get back to this issue later. Qualms about ontological commitments raised 
by substitutional semantics are not deeply relevant for the argument under discussion, for 
one of the premises excludes the possibility that the semantics for QNL ε be substitutional 
and the argument rests on the belief that standard, referential, set–theoretic semantics 
for QNL ε commits one to sets. 
The next premise is: 
(7.36) 
The logic of plurals (and QNL ε for that matter) can have only two 
kinds of semantic: substitutional or set–theoretic. 
What are we to make of this claim? Instead of getting into a philosophical discussion 
regarding non-standard interpretation of the logic of plurals, let us just charitably note 
that these two semantics are usually thought to be two main candidates to the position 
of a formal account of how LP is to be understood. In other words, let us just grant 
this assumption.18 This approach towards the distinction between the substitutional and 
logic without taking it too seriously. This would provide him with interesting mathematical considera­
tions, which would be nevertheless devoid of explanatory power as far as the meaning that he attaches 
to higher–order quantiﬁers goes. 
18In the substitutional semantics a model can be taken to be a set of substituents (thought of as 
constants that can be substituted for variables) together with a valuation function that determines the 
truth–value of atomic sentences constructed from available predicates and terms belonging to the class 
of substituents. Then, the quantiﬁcation is explained in terms of truth–value of those atomic sentences. 
For instance, ∃aa ε a would be true if there were a term t in the class of substituents such that the value 
of t ε t would be Truth. It is not really obvious if this sort of substitutional semantics really explains the 
truth–conditions of sentences. I feel some sympathy for Quine’s remark about substitutional semantics: 
“Ontology is thus meaningless for a theory whose only quantiﬁcation is substitutionally construed; 
meaningless, that is, insofar, as the theory is considered in and of itself. The question of its ontology 
makes sense only relative to some translation of the theory into a background theory in which we use 
referential quantiﬁcation.” (Quine 1968: 210) Perhaps, a translation into a language with referential 
quantiﬁcation is not needed, but certainly I understand how a semantics which does not explain truth–

the referential interpretation of quantiﬁers is quite common. For instance Kearns (1969: 
165–166) says: 
I feel that one can distinguish two fundamentally diﬀerent ways of regarding 
variables– I will call these two views of variables. The ﬁrst view I call the 
Russell–Quine view; the second is the Frege–Le´sniewski view (these will be 
abbreviated as R–Q and F–L, respectively). These two are not the only pos­

variants of one or the other, or perhaps combinations of the two. . . The R–Q 
view of variables could be called the pronoun view of variables. Professor 
Quine has compared the use of variables with many uses of pronouns in En­

R–Q view, quantiﬁers are used to talk about all entities or some entity. . . On 
the F–L view, a variable is seen as a replacement for an expressions. Variables 
do not have ranges of values, where each value is an entity of some kind.19 
Let’s now discuss the last premise: 
(7.37) 
Substitutional semantics for QNL ε is not theoretically satisfactory. 
Why exactly should we accept it? There is a classical paper which discusses substitutional 
interpretation of quantiﬁers in Ontology (K¨ung and Canty 1970). The authors argue that 
Ontology cannot be given substitutional semantics, and since they focus on the part of 
Ontology which resembles QNL ε (i.e. they discuss quantiﬁcation over name variables 
conditions of atomic sentences can be philosophically frustrating. 
19I do not think that Kearns is exactly right crediting the substitutional reading to Le´sniewski; it 
seems that his interpretation relies on a mistranslation. Le´sniewski in the passage Kearns refers to 
explains how he used quantiﬁers when he “did not yet know how to operate with quantiﬁers.” It 
is therefore not sure whether this is how he understood his formal quantiﬁers once he had them. Also, 
he wrote “przy pewnym znaczeniu wyrazu”, which translates as “for some meaning of the expression” 
(Le´sniewski 1927: 203). Kearns translates the phrase as “for some signiﬁcant words” (which would be 
“dla pewnego wyrazu posiadaj¸acego znaczenie” or “dla pewnego znacz¸acego wyrazu” in Polish) and 
takes it to be Le´sniewski’s explanation of his formal quantiﬁers.
