only), their arguments, if compelling, mutatis mutandi apply to QNL ε . How do they 
argue? They discuss those substitutional interpretations that they ﬁnd possible and 
reject them one by one. Here is how they proceed. 
Actual inscriptions. Suppose the quantiﬁcation ranges over actual inscriptions. 
For instance: 
∀a,b(a ε b →∃cc ε a) 
(7.38) 
they read as: “for any two names (nominal inscriptions), if the sentence (sentential 
inscription) in which one name is subject and the other predicate, is true, then there is 
some name contained in a sentence which is true in which this name is the subject and 
a name equiform to the subject of the ﬁrst sentence is the predicate”. 
Note that some sort of equivalence relation (e.g. equiformity) is necessary here. This 
point might be made clearer when we consider the following sentence: 
∀a(a ε a ∨¬a ε a) 
If every occurrence of a in the formula was to stand for one and the same inscription 
the sentence would be true under no substitution, for no single token can occur at more 
than one place. It also seems that the way the authors read the formula is still ﬂawed. 
For suppose we have a name a which does not occur in any sentence. In that case there 
is no such a thing as the sentence in which a is a subject. “Stand–alone” names would 
lie outside the range of quantiﬁcation on this reading. Probably what they had in mind 
was more like ‘for any two names a and b, if an actual sentence a0 ε b0 is true and a0 is 
equiform to a and b0 is equiform to b, then there is a name c such that there is a true 
sentence c ε a00 and a00 is equiform to a. Also, we have to be aware of the usual qualms 
about equiformity.20 If the quantiﬁed variables refer to equiform names indistinctively 
(i.e. for any formula of the language φ(a) its substitution instance φ(t) is true if and only 
20The authors do not deﬁne the notion and take it to be primitive. 

236 
if for any t0 which is equiform with t, the substitution instance φ(t0) is true), the language 
cannot contain names that are equiform but diﬀer in their reference (which means that 
natural languages are not good candidates). Or, one would have to construct a more 
ﬁne–grained relation of equiformity, where only those “geometrically equiform” names 
which have the same “lexical entries” can be really equiform.21 Let’s not dwell on this 
now, suppose that we have answered the qualms about equiformity. Then, still, as the 
authors claim, we run into troubles. 
The fact that a certain actually written sentence logically implies a sentence (which 
in this reading requires that there be a certain inscription) does not ensure that such a 
sentence indeed exist. For instance, even if a sentence mentioned in the antecedent of 
(7.38) exists (for instance, ‘Russell ε philosopher’, if we grant that we can take substi­
tution instances from natural languages), it does not follow that there actually exists a 
sentence which would result from ‘c ε Russell’ by substituting a name for c. 
A slight modiﬁcation of this reading would not require the existence of sentences. It 
would be enough that name inscriptions exist and refer. For instance, (7.38) would be 
rendered as: for any actually existing name inscriptions a and b, if a ε b (that is, if a refers 
to a unique object which is among the objects to which b refers), then there is an actual 
name inscription c such that it refers to a unique object which is one of the objects to 
which a refers. On this reading, the formula would be valid (because the existence claim 
in the consequent is warranted by the existence of a itself). This certainly simpliﬁes 
the semantics and ﬁxes some of the diﬃculties. The problem is that any theory which 
requires that the domain be at least denumerable (like Peano arithmetic, for instance) 
will fail on this reading since the number of existing inscriptions is very likely ﬁnite.22 
Since a very important theoretical purpose of logical systems like Ontology (or systems 
21That is, being “geometrically equiform” would be a necessary but not a suﬃcient condition. The 
necessary and suﬃcient condition for two expressions to be equiform would be something like “they are 
geometrically equiform and they are synonymous”. 
22Unless we accept wild philosophical views, like Lewis’s modal realism or something similar. 

237 
based on the logic of plurals) is to develop a formalization of mathematical discourse, 
if we give QNL ε a substitutional semantics of this sort it will fall short of providing us 
with the framework that we need. 
Logically possible inscriptions. Having abandoned the actualist substitutional 
reading the authors then consider the idea that Le´sniewskian quantiﬁers range over “log­
ically possible inscriptions” (p. 174). An existentially quantiﬁed statement would be 
satisﬁed iﬀ there were a logically possible inscription which would satisfy the require­
ments put on it by the content of the formula. As they quite correctly notice, there is a 
problem with explicating the notion of logically possible inscriptions. They make a valid 
negative point that logical possibility should not be identiﬁed with physical possibility.23 
As for the positive part, they focus ﬁrst on the reading on which logically possible names 
are those that either actually exist or can be introduced according to the Le´sniewskian 
rule of deﬁnition.24 This initially plausible reading is nevertheless not very satisfactory: 
But in ontology proper only two name constants are deﬁnable, the empty 
name and the universal name, just as in Boolean algebra only two non– 
23In a way, Le´sniewski’s metalogic seems to require a modal approach as well. (Kearns 1962: 11) 
says about Le´sniewski’s terminological explanations (which I discuss in section 4.6): ‘The T.E.s are like 
blueprints — they present the rules for as many systems as one cares to construct. For Lesniewski, 
a formal system exists in space and time, and it contains just those results which have actually been 
deduced. This means that there are no unproved theorems of a formal system. However, such a claim 
does not make proofs of completeness and consistency either trivial or senseless. For these must be 
understood as proofs about all possible well–formed formulas or theorems, rather than as about some 
ideal individuals. The problem of potentiality, or possibility, is a diﬀerent problem from that relating 
to abstract entities. Potential objects and events are quite as individual as actual ones. To say that 
every well–formed formula which can be constructed will either be provable or contradictory is not to say 
anything which commits one to recognizing the formal system containing the theorems.’ [the emphasis 
is Kearns’] 
24In QNL ε we could introduce a rule of deﬁnition in the following form. First we extend the alphabet 
by a denumerable number of name constants δ1, δ2, . . . and modify the syntax accordingly. Next, we 
enumerate all QNL ε formulas which do not contain name constants and contain exactly one free variable: 
φ1, φ2, . . .. Then we introduce as axioms all formulas of the form: 
∀a[a ε δi ≡ a ε a ∧ φi(a)] 
and in any model we take δi to denote the set of all and only those objects whose singular names would 
satisfy φi. 

238 
equivalent constants are distinguishable. (p. 177) 
Depending on how one distinguishes name constants, the claim may be true or false. 
On one reading, there are only two constants deﬁnable (suppose we reasonably extend 
S-semantics to allow constants to denote) if in any S-model any deﬁnable constant will 
denote one of certain two subsets of the domain. For instance, if in any model any deﬁned 
constant will denote the empty set or the whole domain of the model, we can (in the 
sense just speciﬁed) say that at most two constants are deﬁnable. In this sense, the above 
claim is true. On the other hand, if what we mean by ‘diﬀerent constants’ is ‘constants 
which are not logically equivalent’, that is, constants such that there is a model where 
they denote diﬀerent subsets of the domain, there are more than two constants. It is the 
second meaning of ‘diﬀerence between constants’ that led Rickey (1985: 186) to criticize 
the above statement. Let us take a look at his argument. 
He suggests that there are other, non–equivalent constants that can be deﬁned in 
Ontology (which are also deﬁnable in QNL ε ). His example is: 
∀a(a ε 2+ ≡ a ε a ∧∃b,c(a ε b ∧ a ε c ∧¬ b ◦ c)) 
(7.39) 
where b
c stands for ∀d(d ε b ≡ d ε c). Further, he claims the following. Take a model 
◦ 
which contains exactly three objects (say, 1, 2, 3) such that a refers to exactly one of 
them (say, 2), b refers to two objects: 1 and 2, and c refers to two objects: 2 and 3. Then, 
he insists, 2+ is a constant diﬀerent from both: the empty (Λ) and the full (V ) name. 
The adequacy of this claim depends on what is meant by ‘distinct constants’. First, 
constants that have diﬀerent shape can be introduced which have logically equivalent 
deﬁnientes. This diﬀerence, however is not strong enough to provide us with the expres­
sivity we need. Second, constants that have deﬁnientes which are not logically equivalent 
can also be introduced (and here ﬁts the diﬀerence between 2+ and Λ). The initial worry 
about the fact that only Λ and V are deﬁnable was that on this sort of substitutional read­

239 
ing the satisfaction of complex formulas will depend only on what is true or false about 
the empty set or about the whole domain, that is, only the empty set or the whole domain 
would, in a sense, lie in the range of name variables (in a sense, this is the semantical 
version of the claim that there are not enough constants). The question is: do constants 
introduced according to Rickey’s recipe (2+ , 3+ , 4+, etc.) ﬁx the problem? Well, what 
objects exactly fall under 2+ in Rickey’s model? In the substitutional interpretation it 
is somewhat hard to decide what this question means.25 A plausible reading is this. On 
this understanding of substitutional interpretation, an object x from the model described 
by Rickey falls under 2+ in that model if and only if, if we introduce a singular term a 
denoting an object x, then the description of the model makes a ε 2+ true. Let’s extend 
the language with singular terms: ‘1’, ‘2’ and ‘3’ denoting objects 1, 2 and 3 respectively. 
Take 1. Certainly 1 ε 1. Also, 1 ε b and 1 ε V . Thus ∃b,c(a ε b ∧ a ε c ∧¬ b
c). Hence
◦ 
1 ε 2+ . Similar arguments work for 2 and 3. Thus, in Rickey’s model 2+ has the same 
denotation as V . In general, any constant of the kind described by Rickey of the form n+ 
(where is a natural number) will denote the same as V in any model which has at least 
n elements and the same as Λ in any model which has less than n elements. Extending 
the language with logically non-equivalent constants deﬁned in accordance with Rickey’s 
recipe does not provide us with a way out. 
K¨ung and Canty propose a yet wider notion of logical possibility of constants. First, 
they remark that even though certain constants cannot be deﬁned in the language of 
Ontology, they can be introduced axiomatically. 
. . . new constants can be added to Ontology not only by means of deﬁnitions, 
but also by means of new axioms (K¨ung and Canty 1970: 177) 
25The problem here is that on the classical reading “substitutional quantiﬁcation is not grounded 
in assignments of objects at all.” (Marcus 1972: 248) Rickey, on the other hand, quite interestingly, 
wants to have his pie and eat it: to interpret quantiﬁcation substitutionally but assign denotation to 
substituents. It is not impossible (in fact, I will be trying to do something similar), but this makes the 
semantics far from being purely substitutional. 

240 
This of course is a little bit tricky, because it is not obvious what conditions have to 
be put on an axiomatic system in order for it to really introduce a new constant. If 
what one cares about is only that introduction of new constants preserves consistency it 
is enough to add axioms saying that a new constant is not coextensive with any other 
constant. If, on the other hand, one believes that a new constant is really introduced if 
the axioms together with rules of inference determine the semantical interpretation of the 
new expression, things can get fairly complex. Fortunately we will not have to get into 
this issue too far, because, arguably, no matter how exactly axioms will introduce new 
constants, we still will run out of constants. We may safely assume that any constant 
would be introduced my means of a single formula or a single axiom schema (say, the 
conjunction of those axioms and/or axiom schemas which would introduce it). But this 
implies there are at most denumerably many formulas (or schemas) that introduce a 
new constant. This indicates that it will be hard to run certain mathematical theories. 
For instance, it will be very hard to mimic the quantiﬁcation over real numbers using 
this sort of substitutional reading. Also, even if the domain of objects is denumerable, 
the substitutional interpretation will give us a diﬀerent result than the set–theoretic 
one, because there will be more subsets of the domain than possible names. Let us 
call the arguments against any substitutional interpretation of higher–order (or plural) 
quantiﬁcation which in one way or another suggest that since the class of substituents 
is at most countable (and this constitutes a problem because we do not have any upper 
limit on the size of the domain of objects to be named) size limitation objections. 
Here, K¨ung and Canty end the list of what they consider acceptable approaches to 
the substitutional quantiﬁcation26 and go on to suggest that. . . 
26I discussed their arguments because they focus on arguments against substitutional interpretation 
of higher order logics speciﬁcally and they cover most of the interpretations that usually come to one’s 
mind. As for general qualms about substitutional interpretation I believe they have been dealt with by 
Kripke (1976). I will just allow myself to discuss brieﬂy an objection raised against the substitutional 
interpretation (not necessarily higher–order) by Tomberlin (1997). The argument is intriguing, and 
it is interesting to see how languages based on QNL ε avoid the diﬃculty. It goes (p. 160): On the 

241 
one must further consider the set of all subsets of that domain. Here, one 
assigns to each constant from the category of names some element from this 
set: call it the extension of the name to which it is assigned. Under any in­
terpretation of ontology, every name is to have some extensions . . . the names 
do not denote their extensions, they merely have their extensions. (p 178) 
and this is where we leave them. The problem is that extensions thus understood are 
prima facie nominalistically unacceptable, and the authors do not provide any argument 
to the eﬀect that they are not.27 
The last premise (i.e. (7.37)) of the semantical argument is the one that I will reject. 
To do that, I will provide an explanation of quite a clear and intuitive notion of possibility 
which provides us with a suﬃcient repertoire of possible names and I provide a viable 
substitutional interpretation. Before doing that, I will discuss two other proposals that 
substitutional reading a sentence of the form ∀xF (x) is true iﬀ all substitution instances of Fx are true 
(where F is given a ﬁxed interpretation). But ‘the unique non–F’ is a legitimate substituent (terms do not 
have to be non–empty to be rightful substituents). Unfortunately, the sentence ‘F (the unique non–F )’ 
will always be false. Hence, the sentence ‘∀xF (x)’ will never be true, because it will always have at least 
one false substitution instance. One might object to this version of the argument on the grounds that 
deﬁnite descriptions are incomplete symbols and as such are not legitimate substituents. Tomberlin has 
a rejoinder here: he can introduce ‘Scotty’ as a non–referential name, and ﬁx its denotation, if it were 
to have one as the one and only individual that is not F and run the argument with ‘Scotty’ instead of 
the deﬁnite description that he used originally. Nevertheless, it seems that the problem does not arise 
for QNL ε even if we admit deﬁnite descriptions as rightful substituents. 
First, how do we express the sentence that all objects have a certain property, or, when we speak 
QNL ε , that all objects fall under a certain name, say a? Certainly not by ‘ ∀bb ε a’, because this 
will always be false (the substitution instance ‘Λ ε a’ is false). Rather, we say that any object is a, 
‘ ∀b(b ε b → b ε a)’. But consider the substitution instance that we get when we substitute ‘the unique 
non–a’ for b: 
If the unique non–a is the unique non–a, then the unique non–a is a. 
If there are no objects which are not a (or if there are at least two such objects), the descriptions fails 
to denote and the sentence comes out vacuously true. If, on the other hand, there is a unique object 
which is not a, then the sentence also gets the correct truth–value, for it is true that the unique non–a 
is the unique non–a but false that it is a. 
27They remark that “A Le´sniewskian system . . . has the advantage that it is at least as powerful as any 
Russellian system without admitting sets into the universe of discourse; that is, without putting, say, 
real things and sets . . . into the same universe. The sets are here recognized as purely linguistic entities, 
which play their role as extensions in the range of quantiﬁcation, not in the universe of discourse.” 
(p. 179) but this looks more like mere verbalism than like a real explanation. The reader is still left 
with the idea that sets have to be in the range in quantiﬁcation and has no idea as to how they are to 
be explained away by saying that they are ‘purely linguistic entities’. 

242 
quite naturally lead to the approach I want to suggest. 
7.4 A modal alternative 
In this section I will ﬁrst present and discuss two approaches to plural quantiﬁcation which 
were meant to provide semantical insight into the logic in question without bringing in 
ontological commitment to abstract objects. Next, I will develop a semantics (motivated 
by these attempts) which avoids the diﬃculties that will be raised against these two 
approaches. 
7.4.1 Simons on combinatorial semantics 
Simons (1985b) suggested that we can think of Le´sniewskian quantiﬁers as ranging over 
ways of meaning (a phrase he coined). He did not deﬁne the notion of a way of 
meaning, but he certainly tried to clarify it. The intuition is that a way of meaning of an 
expression is that part of the semantical character of this expression which contributes to 
the truth of complex sentences (of the extensional language of Ontology). Two examples: 
the way of meaning of ‘Rafal Urbaniak’ is that it refers to only one object, me. The way 
of meaning of the Boolean conjunction is that only when applied to two true sentences 
it yields a true sentence. 
First of all, we are told, in the present context we have to consider only extensional 
aspects of ways of meaning. Hence his ﬁrst postulate: 
(7.40) 
Coextensive expressions mean in the same way. 
Also, the grammatical diﬀerences are supposed to “represent” diﬀerences in reference 
(denotation): 
(7.41) 
No two expressions of diﬀerent semantic category can mean in the 

243 
28
same way.
In other words, two expressions which could mean in the same way, possess the same 
mode of meaning and expressions in diﬀerent sematic category never have the same 
mode of meaning (Two expressions can have the same mode of meaning and yet have 
diﬀerent ways of meaning). 
(7.42) Expressions have the same mode of meaning iﬀ they are of the same 
semantic category. 
(7.42) is not a deﬁnition. It only speciﬁes that the way Ontology is constructed its 
grammar mirrors the distinction between diﬀerent modes of meaning. Then we have two 
principles associated with compositionality: 
(7.43) 
The way of meaning of a complex expression is determined by the 
ways of meaning of its constituents. 
(7.44) 
The mode of meaning of a functor expression is determined by the 
modes of meaning of its arguments and values. 
Now Simons has to specify the mode of meaning of expressions of primitive syntactic 
categories: 
(7.45) 
The mode of meaning of sentences is to be true or false 
(7.46) 
The mode of meaning of names is to designate. 
Among the ways of meaning that names can have, Simons distinguishes certain sub-
modes; there are lots of submodes but those interesting from the present perspective 
are designating no/at least n/exactly n/at most n/all objects, etc. 
28Simons is using Le´sniewski’s terminology, so he speaks of ‘semantic category’. In Ontology, however, 
the category of an expression is determined syntactically. Le´sniewski called it ‘semantic’ because he 
introduced syntactic diﬀerences in order to mirror diﬀerences in what he considered the semantic role of 
expressions. 

244 
An example. Suppose we consider an expression which syntactically is a two–place 
propositional connective (or suppose we are considering a variable of the same syntactic 
category and ask ourselves what the range of quantiﬁcation is). How many ways of 
meaning does it have? Well, a way of meaning for a propositional connective is speciﬁed 
by its output for each possible input, we are told. There are 2n possible inputs, and 
since there are two possible outputs, there are 22n possible ways of meaning of such a 
propositional connective. 
The way of meaning of a name constant is speciﬁed by determining what objects it 
refers to. Simons represents ways of meaning of expressions of propositional logic by 
means of tables (and this is doable, because they would always be ﬁnite objects), and 
suggests that this method can be somehow extended to ways of meaning that names 
have. For this purpose he introduces designation tables. For instance, if there are two 
objects in the domain: A, B there are four ways a name could mean and we can represent 
those ways of meaning by a, b,—, and ab respectively:

The idea is that quantiﬁcation ranges over ways of meanings (e.g. if a is the only free 
variable in φ(a), the sentence ∀aφ(a) is satisﬁed in a model if and only if for any way of 
meaning a can have φ(a) is true in that model), Simons suggests, and such quantiﬁcation 
does not commit one to the existence of objects which are ways of meaning. Simons, 
somewhat mysteriously, remarks: 
Note again that the inputs and outputs of these semantic tables are not 
expressions or any other objects connected directly with a particular language. 
They are ways of meaning. (Simons 1985b: 208) 

246 
He believes that ways of meaning cannot be actual objects, because “there are more 
ways of meaning than there objects to be named” (p. 212). The argument is not deeply 
satisfactory. It seems that a certain ambiguity is involved. In one sense an object has 
to be an element of the domain of things that names can name. On this reading, we 
probably do not want to treat ways of meanings as objects, that is, we do not want to 
consider possible names which name ways of meaning as admissible substituents for name 
variables. In the other sense (which probably is more essential here) ways of meaning are 
actual objects if they really exist. The claim that ways of meaning are not objects in the 
ﬁrst sense (likely true) does not entail that they are not objects in the second sense. The 
ﬁrst claim is only about a certain limit on what names can refer to in a certain (possibly 
formal) language. The second claim is general and metaphysical. 
Even if we admit that ways of meaning are not objects, we are left wondering: what 
are they? A few postulates given by Simons or the fact that some of them (those which 
are generated from a ﬁnite domain of “urelements”) can be represented by a table do not 
provide a philosophical explanation of their status. There is also a technical diﬃculty 
here. Unless we represent tables set–theoretically, a formal semantics which would employ 
tables that list objects, inputs and outputs would be quite unmanageable. 
What is more, even if we assume that tables adequately represent ways names could 
mean in a model, it is impossible to express the meaning of ε. For note that the best 
that a table can do is to express the way ε means (and this works also only in ﬁnite 
models). But what we want to have is not a description of ε relativized to a particular 
model. We want to understand ε independently of our choice of models. This meaning, 
however, cannot be expressed using the table method. 

247 
7.4.2 Rickey’s natural models 
After a simple reformulation (accommodating it to QNL ε ), Rickey’s natural semantics 
(Rickey 1985) is a model theory for QNL ε which, he believed, does not commit one to 
sets when one uses the system. The basic idea is that we take names to belong to a 
distinct set of objects existing in the model and denotation relation to be given together 
with the world. More speciﬁcally, Rickey’s natural model is a tuple hi, n, ∼i, where i 
is a (possibly empty) set of individuals in the world, n is a non–empty set of names in 
the world and ∼⊆ n × i. A natural interpretation of a formula φ is a natural model 
together with a valuation function v which maps name variables occurring in φ into n. 29 
Let I = hi, n, ∼i. The deﬁnition of satisfaction (relative to a valuation function) is: 
hI, vi
|= a ε b iﬀ ∃!x∈iv(a) ∼ x ∧∃x∈i(v(a) ∼ x ∧ v(b) ∼ x) 
(7.47) 
hI, vi
|= ¬φ iﬀ hI, vi 6|= φ 
(7.48) 
hI, vi
|= φ ∧ ψ iﬀ hI, vi |= φ and hI, vi |= ψ 
(7.49)

|= ∀aφ(a) iﬀ hI, v0i |= φ(b) 
where b is a variable which does not occur in φ, and 
v0 extends v by mapping b into n as well. 
(7.50) 
Rickey constructed interpretations of this sort to criticize K¨ung and Canty (1970) by 
showing that substitutional interpretations can have an inﬁnite domain of individuals and 
nevertheless the domain of names does not have to be uncountable to provide suﬃcient 
“substitutional” foundation for higher–order quantiﬁcation. 
A technical remark ﬁrst. As the semantics stands, it does not avoid the diﬃculties 
raised by K¨ung and Canty. For even an extended valuation function v will map new 
variables into the plain old n with the ∼ already ﬁxed. But this means that such an 
29This is the reason why Rickey postulated that n be non–empty. He needed at least one object to 
map name variables to. 

248 
interpretation is (modulo satisﬁability) equivalent to a set–theoretic interpretation where 
only sets generated by the names present in n and by the ∼ relation are in the range 
of quantiﬁcation. That is, only extensions of those names that actually belong to n will 
matter for the satisfaction of formulas, and this is clearly a feature that K¨ung and Canty 
would ﬁnd problematic. In other words, what we get is a semantics equivalent to non– 
standard set–theoretic semantics for higher–order logic, and that means that we will not 
have certain properties that higher–order logic usually is expected to have (incomplete­
ness, categoricity etc.) 
Also, the satisfaction conditions do not seem right if ∼ is not allowed to vary. If, for 
example, in I all names are ∼-related to one and the same object, no extension of v to 
a new name variable will allow a name to refer to something else than that object.30 
Perhaps it would agree more with Rickey’s intentions to say that ∀aφ(a) is satisﬁed 
in a model I = hi, n, ∼i under a valuation v if and only if φ(a) is satisﬁed in any model 
I0 = hi, n0, ∼0i (where n0 = n ∪{y} and y is a new element of n0 and ∼0 extends ∼) under 
the valuation v0 that assigns this new element y of n0 to a. 31 
Rickey did not give any clear argument to the eﬀect that QNL ε with his semantics 
does not carry ontological commitment to sets. A plausible case for this claim, however, 
can be made. First, the quantiﬁcation itself does not bring a commitment to sets. It is 
substitutional and names are individual objects taken to exist in a world pretty much 
just like individuals. Second, the way that names contribute to the truth conditions 
of atomic sentences is non–committal either. 
Names on this view do not refer to 
objects via sets. They simply refer to objects directly. Saying that a name refers 
to objects (e.g. that ‘dog’ refers to dogs) is not the same as saying that it names a unique 
set whose members fall under this name (e.g. that ‘dog’ names a unique set whose all 
30I owe this remark to Richard Zach. 
31Another option would be to avoid extending the set of names and just vary ∼. This would yield the 
same result. I prefer to extend the set of names in order to make this semantics more similar to the one 
I develop (and when I do that, I give a reason why extending the set of names is more intuitive). 

249 
and only elements are dogs and dogs fall under the name ‘dog’ in virtue of belonging to 
this set).32 
As noted above, the semantics, as it is, is not devoid of certain technical diﬃculties. 
In what follows I develop a semantics which avoids the technical obstacles encountered 
by Rickey’s semantics, imports the modal factor suggested by Simons, and provides a 
more general ground for the future developments. 
7.4.3 Kripke semantics for QNL ε 
We have seen that to avoid the ontological commitment of higher–order quantiﬁcation 
Simons suggested that it ranges over ways of meaning. The problem is that this idea is 
quite vague and his table–method does not provide a theory that would count as a formal 
semantics. Rickey, on the other hand, suggested that name constants should be given as 
elements of the model, existing pretty much as individuals, together with a denotation 
relation. The weakness here was that the way he accounted for quantiﬁcation prima facie 
does not satisfy the requirement that the resulting system should be equivalent to the 
one with set-theoretic semantics. Also, he does not exactly indicate how to generalize 
his strategy to obtain a stronger and more expressive system. 
Simons’s account brings in a modal factor. He speaks of ways names could mean.33 
Rickey, on the other hand, suggests that quantiﬁcation over subsets of a domain can be 
avoided by introducing models with two sorts of objects and one binary relation.34 Let 
32A similar point has been made by Boolos (1998a), where the reader can look for a more emphatic 
and detailed explanation of the diﬀerence. 
33“. . . talk about ways is frequently modal in character. It need not be — we can consider the ways in 
which something actually has occurred, but in the problems of combinatorics it is fairly clear that most 
of the ways considered are never put into practice or instantiated at all – we are considering possible 
ways. This is evidenced also by the language in which such problems are posed: “In how many ways 
can . . . ?” This would also help explain why ways may outrun objects, for there could be more objects 
than there in fact are.” (Simons 1985b: 213) 
34It has been argued that any model theory for classical logic employs a modal factor. 
Melvin and Mendelsohn (1998: 11) write: “The notion of possible worlds is of a piece with standard 
model–theoretic interpretations of classical logic. . . A valid formula (i.e. a logical truth) of classical 
propositional logic is one that comes out true for every possible assignment of truth values to the state­

250 
us combine these two ideas. 
The Kripke models deﬁned below will introduce the notion of ways name could be 
formally in a fairly well–known framework. This will allows us to get a better handle on 
the semantics and perhaps provide us with a better understanding of this notion. 
Suppose we start with a world which for our purposes is devoid of linguistic objects 
(especially names). The content of this world is our domain of (already existing) bare 
individuals, call it the bare world. In this world it is possible to introduce countable 
name phrases (names, for short) which would either be empty or refer to one or more 
already existing objects. In this setting we can take a possible world to consist of two 
sorts of objects: bare individuals (those which do not name anything) and names that 
either do not name anything or name (one or more) pure individuals. The situation in 
a possible world determines what reference the names that exist in it have (if there are 
any). The bare world can be also interpreted as such a possible world, only at least one 
of the sorts would be empty. Starting with the bare world we can subsequently extend 
its repertoire of names by introducing (countably many) new names that refer to bare 
individuals. Let us idealize here: we are not putting any restrictions on which individuals 
can be named by a name, we assume that any bare individual (or any bare individuals) 
is (are) nameable. This gives raise to the so–called naming structure: 
Deﬁnition 6. A naming structure is a tuple hI, W i where I is a set (of bare indi­
viduals) and W is a set of possible worlds. A possible world is a tuple hN, δi where 
I ∩ N = ∅ and δ ⊆ N × I. The bare world is the possible world with N = ∅. The 
ment letters. This notion of alternative interpretations should not pose any more of a philosophical 
problem in modal logic than it does in classical logic. Put a slightly diﬀerent way, in so far as there 
are philosophical diﬃculties with the notion of possible world, these are diﬃculties that are already to 
be found in understanding multiple interpretations for statements of classical logic.” I tend to disagree. 
The Platonist can employ the explicit quantiﬁcation over interpretations or models of formulas of clas­
sical logic without using modal notions. Instead of saying ‘for any possible truth–value assignment’ it 
is enough to say ‘for any truth–value assignment’ if truth–value assignments are already deﬁned to be 
functions of certain speciﬁc kind.

following conditions all have to be satisﬁed: 
• B = h∅, ∅i ∈ W (i.e. the naming structure contains the bare world). 
for any w = hN, δi = B: 
– N is countable, and 
– N = ∅. 
• The accessibility relation on possible worlds is deﬁned by the following condition. Let

– N ⊂ N 0, 
– {hx, yi | x ∈ N ∧hx, yi ∈ δ0} = δ. 
If M is a naming structure and I is the set of its bare individuals, we say that I 
underlies M or that M is based on I. If M = hI, W i and w ∈ W we will sometimes 
write w ∈ M. 
A few words of explanation here. First, we start “constructing” a naming structure 
with a bare world. This bare world together with I represents the situation where we 
have a set of objects that can be named but we have not introduced any names yet. Since 
in principle (unless one has very speciﬁc religious beliefs) there is no reason to believe in 
unnameable individuals, the I in the bare worlds is simply the domain of objects. 
For now, we are considering ways individuals which are not names could be named. 
This means that introducing new names does not change the domain of individuals (that 
is why I is a set in the naming structure and does not vary with possible worlds). Also, 
this indicates that the only way we get from one possible world to another accessible 
world is by extending the repertoire of available names (so N has to be a proper subset 
of N 0). 

252 
On the nominalist reading, quite plausibly, names are ﬁnite sequences of symbols (or 
phonemes) from a ﬁnite alphabet. This means that in any possible world there can be 
at most countably many names (hence the requirement that N be countable). Moreover, 
the basic idea is that by “going” to another possible world we are extending what we 
already have; not changing the ways that names that we already have are. That is, we 
can add a new name and take it to refer to such–and–such objects, but the reference of 
the already existing names cannot be changed. In a sense, names are thought of as given 
with reference. Hence the denotation relation in an accessible world has to agree with 
the initial denotation relation on all names that already existed before we extended the 
set of names. 
This sort of structure will not ensure yet that every intuitively possible way of meaning 
will have a representation in a model. To do this, we have to require that for any subset 
of the domain of individuals in a possible world there be an accessible possible world in 
which there is a name which denotes all and only its elements. 
Deﬁnition 7. Let hN, δi = w ∈ W . A naming structure M = hI, W i is w–complete if 
and only if:

A naming structure M = hI, W i is complete iﬀ for any w ∈ W M is w–complete.  
The basic idea is that a naming structure is w–complete iﬀ for any set of individuals 
existing in this world, there is a world accessible from w where a name which names 
elements of this set exists. In other words, it is w–complete if it models the full range of 
ways names could be in w.

The reason why B–completeness is not suﬃcient is that the class of B–complete 
naming structures would agree with classical set–theoretic semantics only on sentences 
with only one quantiﬁer. In a naming structure, the iteration of quantiﬁers carries us 
deeper and deeper into the structure (this will become clearer when I introduce the modal 
semantics based on naming structures). 
I can now explain how quantiﬁcation in QNL ε is supposed to range over ways names 
could be (or refer). I will be evaluating sentences in a naming structure by evaluating 
them in its bare world. First, we have to deﬁne satisfaction of a formula at a world in a 
naming structure.

The basic idea is that we treat the class of names that exist in a certain world as 
the class of substituents when we evaluate a formula in this world. Since the bare world 
does not contain names the notion of satisfaction in the bare world as applied to open 
formulas is not very fascinating. No formula containing free variables will be satisﬁed at 
the bare world. The situation changes when it comes to evaluating sentences in bare 
worlds. First, let us say that a sentence is true in a naming structure M if and 
only if it is satisﬁed in its bare world under any valuation. A sentence is M–valid if 
and only if it is true in any naming structure. A sentence is M–complete valid if it is 
true in any complete naming structure. 

255 
Theorem 12. For any QNL ε sentence φ, φ is S–valid if and only if φ is M–complete 
valid. 
Proof. Since we are talking about truth of sentences at the bare world, and this depends 
on the satisfaction of formulas at non-bare worlds, it will be enough to show that (i) for 
any naming structure, any non-bare world in it and any valuation, there is an S-model 
that agrees with this possible world under that valuation on any QNL ε-formula, and (ii) 
for any formula that fails at an S-model w there is a naming structure with a certain 
non-bare world and a valuation, so that φ fails at that world in this naming structure 
under that valuation. 
ad (i). I will just show, given a non-bare world in a naming structure and a valuation 
function, how to construct a corresponding S-model that satisﬁes exactly those formulas 
that are satisﬁed at that world under that valuation. Take a w = hN, δi in a naming 
structure based on I. Let v map name variables a1, a2, . . . into N. To obtain an equivalent 
S-model w0 = hD, Ini take D = I and let for any ai: 
In(ai) = {x ∈ I | δ(v(ai), x)}

0 , i.e. such that for any i: 
∀x∈I [δ3(v3(ai), x) ≡ x ∈ In0

But v3 will diﬀer from v at most at a, so also w |= ∃aφ. 
ad (ii). Suppose a formula φ fails at an S-model M = hD, Ini, let a1, . . . ak be all 
variables that occur in φ. Take a naming structure w with I = D. Since the structure is 
complete, there exist a possible world w = hN, δi such that for any A ⊆ D, if for some i, 
In(ai) = A then there is a y ∈ N such that for all x ∈ I, δ(y, x) ≡ x ∈ A. If In(ai) = A 
let v(ai) be an arbitrary y ∈ N for which this condition ( ∀x∈I (δ(y, x) ≡ x ∈ A) holds. 
Then also φ fails at this possible world under v. The proof (that M and w agree on φ) 
goes by induction. Again, the basic case and Boolean steps are quite obvious. For the 
quantiﬁed formulas (say, ∃aψ) notice that if there is an S-model M 0 that diﬀers from M 
on at most one variable then (via the completeness of naming structure) there has to be 
a possible world w0 accessible from w and a valuation function v0 which diﬀers at most 
on this variable, such that w0 agrees with M 0 on ψ under v0. 
Interestingly, various versions of weaker logics are obtainable when we put additional 
restrictions on the accessibility relation. For instance we can require that names name 
only a ﬁnite number of objects, or that the only way a new name can be introduced is by 
means of a deﬁnition; we can be interested in another notion of validity, which depends 
on the class of all naming models, not necessarily complete, and obtain weaker logics 
for which completeness results are available, pretty much just like it happens with for 
instance Henkin semantics etc. Those subsystems lie beyond the scope of the present 
considerations.

|
6
257 
7.4.4 Modal logic of naming 
I gave a modal semantics for QNL ε and I have shown how it preserves classical, set-
theoretic theoremhood. To clarify the issue of ontological commitment of this semantics 
I now develop a ﬁrst-order modal logic of naming which (in a sense to be speciﬁed) 
mimics the behavior of plural quantiﬁers. The natural language reading of the many-
sorted modal formulas I take to express the meaning I intend to assign to QNL ε formulas. 
I will return to the issue of ontological commitment once all technicalities are in place. 
The language of the modal logic of naming (MLN) contains two sorts of variables: 
individual variables 
x, y, z, x1, x2, . . . , y1, y2, . . . , z1, z2, . . . 
and name variables 
n, m, o, n1, n2, . . . , m1, m2, . . . , o1, o2, . . . 
Besides it contains quantiﬁers ranging over objects of those two sorts (∀, ∃), classical 
propositional connectives, two modal operators (, ♦), no predicate variables, identity 
symbol (=) and one two–place predicate constant (Δ). Formation rules are standard 
(the only new thing is that Δ takes name variables as ﬁrst arguments and individual 
variables as second arguments). 
Models of MLN are just naming structures. 
Deﬁnition 10. An MLN–interpretation is a tuple hM, w, ii where M = hI, W i is 
a naming structure, hN, δi = w ∈ M and i is (a) undeﬁned if N = ∅ and (b) maps 
all individual variables into I and all name variables into N otherwise. Satisfaction of 
MLN–formulas in interpretations is deﬁned as follows: 
hM, w, ii = Δ(n, x) iﬀ i is deﬁned (i.e. N = ∅, see (a) above) and hi(n), i(x)i ∈ δ. 
• hM, w, ii |= t1 = t2 iﬀ i(t1) = i(t2), where each ti is a variable of one of the sorts. 

258 
• hM, w, ii |= ¬φ iﬀ hM, w, ii 6|= φ. 
• hM, w, ii |= φ ∧ ψ iﬀ hM, w, ii |= φ and hM, w, ii |= ψ. 
• hM, w, ii |= ∃αφ iﬀ there is an interpretation i0 (mapping individual variables into I and 
name variables into N) that diﬀers from i only in what it assigns to α and hM, w, i0i |= φ. 
An MLN–sentence is true in a naming structure if it is satisﬁed in its underlying 
bare world.  
Intuitively we read ‘♦φ’ as ‘there is a way names could be such that φ’ and ‘Δ(n, x)’ 
as ‘x is one of the objects denoted by n’ or ‘n refers to x’ (where it is not assumed that 
names have to refer uniquely). 
There is a translation from QNL ε into MLN. We may safely assume that all variables 
of QNL ε are of the form ai. Each variable ai is translated as ni. The translation function 
f is deﬁned as follows: 
f(ai ε aj )

It follows from the deﬁnitions of satisfaction and truth for both systems that: 
Theorem 13. For any QNL ε–sentence φ and any naming structure M, M |= QNL ε φ 
(φ is true in M) if and only if M |= MLN f(φ). 
Proof. This relies on the fact that if we just take i(nk) = v(ak) in all worlds but the bare 
world the models behave pretty much in the same way. For take any naming structure 
M = hI, W i and a non-bare world w = hN, δi (we are not concerned with the bare 

259 
world because we are evaluating sentences) take i(nk) = v(ak) for any k. Then, for any 
QNL ε-formula φ, hM, w, vi |= φ if and only if hM, w, ii |= f(φ). 
Consider the basic case. The formula ai ε aj is satisﬁed at w under v if and only if v 
assigns to ai a name which is δ-related to exactly one object and this object is among 
objects to which the name v-assigned to aj is δ-related. But this is the same condition 
(mutatis mutandis, because we speak of function i and not v) which is stated by t(ai ε aj ). 
The steps pertaining to Boolean connectives are simple. Let us turn to the quantiﬁer. 
∃aφ is satisﬁed at w under v iﬀ there is an R-accessible world (from w), w0 such that φ 
is satisﬁed at it under a valuation that diﬀers from v at most at a. But this is essentially 
the same condition as the satisfaction condition on ♦∃ni t(φ). 
7.4.5 Remarks 
Ways names could be and Chihara’s constructibility quantiﬁers. One way to go 
now, if one wants to provide a nominalist story about quantiﬁcation in QNL ε, is to say 
that MLN provides us with a correct rendering of sentences of QNL ε and to claim that 
the possible–worlds semantics employed is just a useful myth which allows one to make the 
relation between the above mentioned modalities and quantiﬁers in QNL ε clear. Prima 
facie, this seems to be the strategy employed by Chihara (1990), whose constructibility 
quantiﬁers play pretty much the same role in his system as QNL ε quantiﬁers in their 
modal interpretation.35 I agree with him that the notion of constructibility (or logical 
admissibility) is primitive, and I agree with his remarks about the existence of possible 
tokens (esp. with the remarks on pp. 40–54) and the role of possible worlds (I am far 
from being a modal realist).36 The language of MLN I take to be quite intuitive and the 
35He writes: “Remember, that for me, this whole possible world structure is an elaborate myth, useful 
for clarifying and explaining modal notions, but a myth just the same. It would be a mistake to take 
this myth too seriously and imagine that we are exploring real worlds, ﬁnding there real open–sentence 
tokens that have puzzling features.” (p. 60) However, he does have much more to say about possible 
worlds and modalities (see Chihara 1998). 
36I do not want to repeat his remarks, so see his works for details. 

260 
MLN–renderings of the usual axioms of QNL ε I take to be intuitively obvious (However, 
I have to put aside any philosophical discussion concerning the qualms about acceptability 
of modalities from the nominalist point of view). The main diﬀerences are: (i) When we 
extend the language to higher–order names (a strategy that lies beyond the scope of the 
present work) the result will not resemble type theory, but rather a cumulative hierarchy, 
and (ii) instead of introducing constructibility quantiﬁers explicitly I model ways names 
could be using standard modalities and reshaping the accessibility relation. 
Are there enough substitutents? We can recall that one of the objections against 
the substitutional interpretation of QNL ε was that if the domain is inﬁnite we will always 
run out of substituents for under no circumstances the existence of uncountable inﬁnity 
of names is possible. Naming structures by deﬁnition can only contain possible worlds 
with a countable number of names. It does not follow, however, that we will run out 
of possible names if the bare world is inﬁnite. In any complete naming structure any 
subset of the domain will have a possible name corresponding to it. Of course, it is not 
the case that there is a possible world which contains all such names simultaneously, but 
that is not a problem for the modal interpretation of higher–order quantiﬁcation. The 
initial plausibility of the objection results from the ambiguity between: 
(7.55) 
It is possible that for every subset of the domain there is a name 
which names all and only those objects which are elements of this subset. 
and 
(7.56) 
For any subset of the domain it is possible that there is a name which 
names all and only all its elements. 
Let us grant that the set of names in a possible world has to be countable; at least on the 
nominalist view names are ﬁnite inscriptions or utterances over a ﬁnite alphabet. The 
diﬀerence between the readings given above is pretty much like the diﬀerence between 

261 
the statement that it is possible that every real number has a name (which would require 
the existence of uncountably many names) and the claim that for any real number it 
is possible for it to have a name. The ﬁrst is false, the second true. Size limitation 
objections may work well against (7.55). Alas, it is not (7.55) but rather (7.56) that is 
posited in the modal interpretation under discussion, and it is not prone to this sort of 
diﬃculties. 
Why complete naming structures? When we try to provide a nominalist story 
about a logic equivalent to QNL ε with S–semantics, what reasons do we have to postulate 
that it is only complete naming structures that matter? In a sense, why do we concern 
ourselves with such a strong notion, one might say? If the claim is that this is what 
nameability really is like, one might launch an attack along the following lines. 
Certain objects are nameable if and only if there is a person who could name 
them. But it is far from obvious that for any objects there is a person who 
can name them. For suppose a person utters (or writes) a token and wants to 
establish its reference. It seems that she either has to be able to formulate in 
her language a description which only those objects satisfy and which employs 
only those expressions whose reference she may take for granted, or she has 
to have direct epistemic access to those objects (well, at least pretty much as 
direct as the act of pointing at an object, as opposed to determining reference 
through a description). But it is not obvious that her language is suﬃciently 
expressive to provide her with a description that determines this set, and it 
is quite obvious that her relation to quite a few objects in the world will be 
far from direct epistemic access. Hence, it seems, we should not attach that 
sort of signiﬁcance to complete naming structures. 
Here is a rejoinder to this sort of criticism. The required notion of nameability does 
not require the existence of persons who can actually name those objects, nor does it 

262 
obviously require that there be an ideal agent who would be able to name certain objects, 
had he existed in this possible world. We can consider objects belonging to a certain set 
nameable if we know that by introducing a name that denotes all and only its elements 
we would not run into troubles of logical nature, and that is all there is to it. A theory of 
nameability thus understood is not a chapter in mathematical theology (if I may borrow 
an expression from (Boolos et al. 2002: 19)) concerned with ideal naming agents and so 
on, but rather a theory which tries to describe certain conditions under which a name 
that denotes certain objects can be introduced without worrying about consistency. Vivid 
metaphors which employ an ideal naming agent may be helpful, but the possible behavior 
of ideal agents is not what the theory is about, strictly speaking. 
An analogy might help. Suppose someone argues: 
Certain functions are computable if and only if there is a person who could 
compute them. But it is far from obvious that for any (say) Turing com­
putable function there is a person who can compute its value for any input. 
Quite obviously, there are certain computable functions and certain inputs 
such that no person alive would be able to compute the values of those func­
tion for those inputs. Therefore we should not attach such signiﬁcance to the 
class of all Turing–computable functions. 
Arguments of this sort do not disprove the usefulness of the notion of Turing–computability. 
At best, they just show that people’s actions are not exactly what Turing computability 
theory is about. Also, even though the notion of computability used here is indeed quite 
strong and therefore it may aid the imagination to think of it as a theory of an ideal com­
puter (in the good old sense of the word, when ‘computer’ meant the same as ‘reckoner’ 
or ‘ﬁgurer’), ideal computing agents are not exactly the subjects of the discipline. 
On the other hand, qualms about the practical intractability of certain Turing com­
putable functions are legitimate and certain other theories bearing upon more manage­

263 
able functions are called for. Likewise, doubts concerning the divergences between the 
practical nameability and the logical nameability can suggest other, diﬀerent, more 
practically interested theories. 
Let me draw another parallel here. Of high importance in Turing computability 
theory are limitation theorems: the knowledge that even if we put pretty much no 
practical restrictions on computability there still are functions which cannot be computed 
is quite substantial. The purpose of the nameability theory is to provide an (in a sense) 
substitutional semantics for QNL ε . Note that when we think of a logic substitutionally 
one of our main concerns is to be able to apply it to any names whatsoever without any 
uncertainty as to whether the validity or truth will be preserved when we eliminate the 
universal quantiﬁer and substitute a name for the variable that it binds. Here, widening 
the range of possible names strengthens our case (in a sense, it makes our logic more 
universal). Of course, it would be enough if we could somehow delimit the class of 
possible names that practically speaking could be introduced and treat those possible 
names as admissible substituents. This would not change the applicability of our logic. 
A dissimilarity between computability and nameability emerges here. We now have 
computers (that is, machines) whose computational capabilities are quite deﬁned and it 
makes practical sense to introduce more practically motivated precise notions of com­
putability (for instance, because problems which are not solvable in polynomial time are 
quite intractable by means nowadays available it made sense to introduce the notion 
of NP complexity). In the case of naming there is no ﬁne line to draw between what 
practically speaking can be named and what cannot (i.e. there does not seem to exist 
a notion that would mirror the relevant sense of ‘intractability‘ in our analogy). For in­
stance, it does not seem proper to postulate that names can denote only a ﬁnite number 
of objects or that the only way new names can be formed is by deﬁnitions employing 
expressions whose reference is already known. For this reason it seems it is better, at 

264 
least for now, to stick with the idealized notion of nameability which does not put any 
practical limitations on naming capability.37 
Against this universality claim one can try to argue that by considering complete 
naming structures only we do not strengthen our logic, but rather do quite the opposite. 
When instead of considering all naming structures we restrict ourselves to complete 
structures only, the argument may go, “we weaken our logic: we lose completeness!” 
Confusing as such a statement may be, it is certainly interesting. Let us take a look 
at what is going on here. The problem will emerge when the system will be extended 
to obtain a system of power comparable to that of full higher–order logic. The set of 
formulas of thus extended language valid with respect to the class of complete naming 
structures (this notion will be also modiﬁed to account for the generality required by the 
system) will not be eﬀectively axiomatizable, whereas had we restricted our attention 
to naming structures without putting this additional requirement, we would be able to 
obtain an eﬀective axiomatization of the system. Now, since eﬀective axiomatizability 
is a “nice feature”, the argument suggests, we should not introduce a semantics which 
would force us to abandon it. 
This sort of objection results from an ambiguity of the word ‘logic’. If we have in 
mind some sort of axiomatization of a theory and by ‘logic’ we mean an axiomatic system, 
then the change of semantics does not change the logic at all (if we do not change our 
inference rules, axioms etc.), although our axiomatic system with respect to the stronger 
semantics (which considers only complete structures) does not have certain (perhaps) 
desirable properties that it had with respect to the weaker semantics. If by ‘logic’ we 
mean the consequence operation (or the validity) determined by a language together with 
37For instance, in a similar spirit Venema (1998: 1196) criticizing Manzano (1996) who focuses on 
Henkin–style semantics for higher–order logic (that is, the type of semantics which does not presume 
that all subsets of the domain are in the range of second–order quantiﬁers) complains that she did not 
give any examples “of how non–standard semantics forms a good modeling for phenomena in the outside 
world”. 

265 
its semantics (as we do in our considerations) then by limiting our attention to complete 
structures we do not weaken the logic. To the contrary, we strengthen it to the level that 
no axiomatic system can match. 
Set-theoretic content of plural logic. On the face of it, if the nominalist can pro­
vide us with a nominalistically acceptable philosophical motivation to treat the modal­
ities involved in the modal translations of sentences of QNL ε as ontologically non– 
committing, the story about modal interpretation of QNL ε given above suggests how 
he can use QNL ε without being committed to abstract objects. 
There is, however, yet another style of arguments against the belief that the logic of 
plurals is ontologically neutral. I mentioned it brieﬂy on page 227. The objection can be 
extracted from an argument against the logicality thesis (“the logic of plurals qualify as 
pure logic”). It is not my present concern to deﬁne (or discover) what being a pure logic 
consists in; let me just say that the main feature of plural logic that struck some people 
as problematic is its apparently set–theoretic content. The claim that LP (or QNL ε, for 
that matter) has a set–theoretic content, together with the initially plausible assumption 
that whatever has set–theoretic content is committed to sets lead to the conclusion that 
LP is committed to sets. 
Here is a good example of a discussion pertaining to the logicality of the logic of 
plurals:38 
First [in the logic of plurals] there are the tautologies and the inference rules 
governing identity and the singular quantiﬁers. There is broad consensus 
that these qualify as logical. Next there are the inference rules governing the 
plural quantiﬁers. Since these rules are completely analogous to the rules 
governing the singular quantiﬁers it would be hard to deny that they too 
38Linnebo has also another argument against the ontological innocence of the logic of plurals (2003). 
Since it rather pertains to higher–order extensions of the logic of plurals, I will get back to this sort of 
objections once I provide a modal account of those more complicated systems. 

266 
qualify as logical. Then there are the extensionality axioms and the axiom 
that all pluralities are non–empty. These axioms are unproblematic because 
they can plausibly be taken to be analytic. What remains are the plural 
comprehension axioms, where things are much less clear. For these axioms 
have no obvious singular counterparts, and their syntactical form indicates 
that they make existential claims.39 (Linnebo 2004) 
Mutatis mutandis, this objection can be mounted against the ontological innocence of 
QNL ε: one might say, it not only makes existential claims (comprehension axioms of the 
form ∃a∀b(b ε a ≡ b ε a ∧ φ(b)) come out true in any complete naming structure), but also 
clearly has non–logical content because the comprehension axioms have no corresponding 
ﬁrst–order (or singular) counterparts. Both qualms deserve consideration. Let us start 
with the ﬁrst one. 
If we believe that nameability is quite well–understood without postulating abstract 
objects and we accept the modal reading of QNL ε quantiﬁers, there is no reason to read 
any serious existential import into comprehension axioms. They just mirror the fact 
that for any formula φ of QNL ε at any possible world w there is no logical obstacle to 
introducing a new name that would refer to those individuals in w which satisfy φ, or 
that names that coincide in reference with one–place open formulas of QNL ε are logically 
admissible.40 
How about singular counterparts of comprehension axioms? It is not wholly clear 
what the objection is supposed to attain if it is meant to show that LP is committed to 
sets. I guess the intuition behind it is this. If something is a fairly obvious generalization 
of what is already present in the standard ﬁrst–order logic, its nature is logical. Thus, for 
39Linnebo does not exactly say that it is his view; nor does he say it is not his view. I just use his 
wording for its brevity and clarity. 
40Say we take φ(a) to refer to those objects whose singular names (had there been any) substituted 
for a in φ(a) (which does not contain other free variables) would yield true sentences. 

267 
instance (at least when it comes to the rules of inference in any standard axiomatization) 
the rules pertaining to behavior of quantiﬁers in QNL ε are on par with the rules per­
taining to quantiﬁers in ﬁrst–order logic. The same goes for rules pertaining to Boolean 
connectives. Comprehension axioms, on the other hand, one might say, are something 
new: there is nothing in ﬁrst order logic which suggests anything like comprehension 
axioms in QNL ε . 
On the modal reading of QNL ε , the comprehension schema may be taken to say 
something about the power of the language within a naming structure. The fact that 
it holds in a naming structure would indicate that the expressive power of forming new 
deﬁnitions in QNL ε does not supersede the nameability: that at least one legitimate way 
of introducing new names is by postulating that they refer to those (already existing) 
objects which satisfy certain condition expressible in the language of QNL ε . What 
meaning would a singular comprehension schema have on the modal reading? Well, 
a plausible interpretation is that it would say something like: for any formula which 
determines exactly one object it is possible to introduce an individual constant which 
names the unique individual that satisﬁes the formula. This, however, cannot be stated 
in the standard ﬁrst–order language because its quantiﬁers are understood literally and it 
has no means of referring to possible names. This should indicate that the comprehension 
axioms are not responsible for extending the ontological commitment of a theory; rather, 
the reason why it is hard to formulate their ﬁrst–order versions and why it is easy to 
formulate QNL ε versions of comprehension axioms is that names in QNL ε can name 
pretty much any objects without restriction, whereas in a ﬁrst–order language we have 
individual variables but no variables ranging over possible individual constants. 
Is the reference relation innocent? Granted that quantiﬁcation over sets has been 
eliminated in virtue of the modal–substitutional interpretation of plural quantiﬁcation, 
one can still raise doubts regarding the innocence of the reference relation that possible 

268 
names bear to objects in the world. On could argue along the following lines:41 
Ok, so we made names the values of variables. How does it help if, it seems, 
the names themselves can still refer to sets via the δ relation? Why isn’t this 
just syntactic sugar? 
To understand the misreading that stands behind this sort of objection let us ﬁrst note 
that there are three, not two, readings involved. One, the straightforwardly set–theoretic 
one reads ‘∃a’ as: 
(7.57) There is a set a such that. . . 
The second one, which is the reading implicit in the above objection interprets ‘∃a’ as: 
(7.58) It is possible to introduce a name which refers to a certain set such 
that. . . 
Of course, this second reading of quantiﬁers, even though, in a sense, substitutional, 
does not eliminate ontological commitment to sets. The natural language reading that 
the modal semantics I developed suggests is none of the above. Rather, it interprets the 
phrase in question as: 
(7.59) It is possible to introduce a name (which may not refer at all, refer 
to a unique object or refer to multiple objects), such that . . . 
The last reading does not interpret possible names as referring to sets. Rather, it assumes 
that the fact that a name (a countable noun phrase) refers to multiple objects or does 
not refer to anything at all is of the same ontologically innocent status as the fact that 
a name refers to a unique object. 
Even the most severe opponents of the ontological innocence of the logic of plurals 
allow that reference of singular terms does not bring in any commitment to sets. So, it 
41Richard Zach turned my attention to this sort of doubt. 

269 
seems, the reference relation alone is not a problem for a nominalist. Now, why should 
this change when he admits that a name can fail to refer to anything? Well, it should 
not. To say that ‘Spiderman’ does not refer to anything is not to say that ‘Spiderman’ 
refers to the empty set. It is only a very prejudiced commitment to the ‘Fido’-Fido 
principle that may convince one that for any expression there has to exist its unique 
referent which explains how the expression contributes to the truth–value of sentences in 
which it occurs. Having said this, let us ask: Why should the situation be diﬀerent when 
we turn to names that refer to more than one object? 
It seems that competent language users which did not have any experience with 
philosophy should have a say in this debate; and I presume that very few of them would 
suggest that ‘Dogs are animals’ is true in virtue of there being certain objects which 
do not exist in space and time42 such that each dog remains in a certain relation of 
“belonging” to one of them and each animal remains in this relation to another and those 
two untouchable objects remain in a certain unobservable relation. To the contrary, the 
fact that underlies the truth of this sentence is quite observable and pertains to certain 
real individual objects: dogs and animals. It requires a certain amount of philosophical 
sophistry to deny that ‘dogs’ refers to dogs or that ‘animals’ refer to animals along the 
lines suggested above. 
When we try to explain nominalistically the logic of plurals, there are two aspects 
that have to be dealt with. First, we should say what plural quantiﬁers quantify over 
(or how they should be interpreted). My reading is: they are modal devices which are 
explained substitutionally in terms of possible countable noun phrases. Second, the very 
use of plurals, even without quantiﬁers should be explained as nominalistically acceptable. 
My explanation is: unless someone assumes that every expression should have a unique 
42For the sake of simplicity I ignore the position that sets exist in space and time in addition to usual 
objects as quite unbelievable. This position was held for a while by Maddy (1980). A plausible criticism 
of it is to be found in (Chihara 1990: 194–215). 

270 
referent and believes in set theory already there is no linguistic evidence to the eﬀect that 
the existence of sets is required to make sense of the reference of plurals. Interpreting 
plurals as referring to sets has certain theoretic advantages when we do formal semantics 
(set–theory is fairly well–known and the semantics is slightly simpler), but this does not 
constitute any argument to the eﬀect that this is the only way to make sense of plural 
constructions. A more parsimonious answer is: plurals refer to objects directly. ‘Dogs’ 
refers to dogs, ‘chairs’ refers to chairs and it is enough to believe in the existence of chairs 
and dogs (as opposed to believing in the existence of sets as well) in order to understand 
sentences in which ‘dogs’ or ‘chairs’ occur. 
The expression ‘refers to’ in the relevant sense has pretty much the same meaning 
as ‘is truly predicable of’. When we introduce a name for already existing objects, it 
refers to those already existing objects of which it is truly predicable. ‘Rafal Urbaniak’ 
refers to me because ‘This is Rafal Urbaniak’ is true when the utterer points at me. 
There is nothing that forces us to say that being–truly–predicable–of in the case of 
‘Rafal Urbaniak’ requires that there be a singleton set containing me as its sole element. 
What is more, clearly if being–truly–predicable–of something is a necessary condition for 
referring to this thing (and this is how I prefer to understand the reference relation), 
‘Rafal Urbaniak’ does not refer to any set (even though being an eternal being might be 
an upside of being a set, I tend to think of myself as an object which is not out of space 
and time). Similarly, if we have an empty name, like ‘Superman’, there is no object of 
which we can truly say that it is Superman (‘Superman’ is not truly predicable of the 
empty set). It is false that in this sense ‘Superman’ refers to the empty set because it 
is false that the empty set is Superman. How about names that intuitively name more 
than one object? The expression ‘chair(s)’ is truly predicable of each and every chair 
and therefore every chair is a referent of the word ‘chair’.43 Also, the set of chairs (if 
43Thus, on the view under discussion, the reference of distributive plurals is nothing above and beyond 

271 
there is such an object) is not a referent of the word ‘chair(s)’, for it is not a chair itself. 
Hence, in this sense of the phrase, reference of names is a relation between tokens and 
individuals themselves which does not proceed via sets and which does not require the 
existence of sets. 
7.4.6 Summary of the chapter 
I ﬁrst constructed a language of the quantiﬁed name logic. Then, I provided it with a set– 
theoretic semantics. For the sake of comparison, I have shown that (modulo set–theoretic 
semantics) we can take it to be a variant of the logic of plurals. Next, I discussed an 
alleged argument to the eﬀect that the logic of plurals bears with it a commitment to 
sets. The argument relies on the assumption that the substitutional interpretation of the 
quantiﬁers does not result in semantics which can satisfy the same theoretical postulates 
which the set–theoretic semantics fulﬁlls. 
I turned to a more detailed analysis of the argument against the substitutional inter­
pretation of plural quantiﬁers. It turns out that in the literature of the subjects there 
have been at least two attempts to construct an interpretation of plural quantiﬁers which 
does not commit one to sets and does not weaken the expressive power of the language in 
question. On of them is not really substitutional: it is based on the idea that quantiﬁers 
range over ‘ways names could mean’. The diﬃculty is that it is not well–worked out and 
the notion of a ‘way name could mean’ is taken to be primitive. Another, in a sense, is 
substitutional, but the problem is it actually changes the logic. 
So I developed a modal semantics which uses possible worlds to clarify the notion of 
a ‘way name could be’ and employs some intuitions behind the substitutional reading 
of quantiﬁers used in the second of the attempts mentioned above. Given that the 
accessibility relation is shaped according to certain obvious intuitions, sentences of the 
the reference to particulars. This means that we also do not need anything like “mereological fusion of 
all chairs” as the referent of ‘chair(s)’ (at least as far as plural quantiﬁcation is concerned). 

272 
quantiﬁed name logic have a reasonable translation into a more familiar language — the 
two–sorted language of ﬁrst–order modal logic. 
This indicates that the ontological commitment of quantiﬁed name logic (on the modal 
reading) does not go beyond the commitment of ﬁrst–order modal logic. The quantiﬁers 
of quantiﬁed name logic range over possible name tokens and the reference relation is 
a relation that bears name tokens directly to extra–linguistic individuals without the 
mediacy of sets.
